# Proxy Season

# Chapter 1

Dan's alarm went off at six-forty, which meant Mira had already left the bedroom. He could tell because the door was open — she always left it open, not as courtesy but as a reflex, done with the room and already moving on. He lay still for a moment, listening. The shower was off. The kitchen faucet ran briefly, then stopped. Theo's voice, muffled, asking something about toast.

He put on his glasses and the room sharpened into the version of itself he'd been living in for six years: the water stain on the ceiling that looked like nothing, the dresser they'd bought on Atlantic Avenue when Theo was a baby, the closet door that didn't close all the way because the frame had shifted. His three suits hung inside. Navy, charcoal, lighter gray. He picked the charcoal because it was Wednesday and Wednesday was charcoal, a system he'd invented without meaning to and now couldn't break.

In the kitchen, Theo sat at the table eating cereal, his sketchbook open beside the bowl. He was drawing something with a blue colored pencil. Dan's old headphones hung around his neck the way they always did — unplugged, purposeless. He'd claimed them two years ago and now couldn't leave the apartment without them. The morning light came through the east-facing window and caught the side of his face — the gap where his two front teeth used to be, the new ones coming in at slightly different speeds so one was a half-step ahead of the other.

"Morning, T."

"Morning." Theo didn't look up. His pencil moved in short, careful strokes along what looked, from Dan's angle, like the edge of a roofline.

Mira was at the counter, dressed already — slim trousers, a dark blouse, her canvas tote bag from graduate school sitting on the chair like a third person at the table. She'd cut her hair three weeks ago, short enough that it changed the shape of her face, and Dan hadn't said anything about it because he couldn't figure out what to say that wouldn't sound like an opinion about her body, which was territory they'd stopped entering some time ago, without discussing it. They'd stopped sharing most things.

"There's coffee," she said.

"Thanks."

He poured himself a cup and stood at the counter drinking it because sitting at the table with both of them felt like something that required a decision he hadn't made yet. Mira was looking at her phone, scrolling through whatever Gemini-state had sent her overnight — the prompts and schedules and appearance requests that constituted her work, while signing things and nodding constituted his. She was better at it. She'd always been better at the performance side of professional life, even before the performances became the only side there was.

"I'm on the early train," she said, not to him exactly but in his direction. "Can you walk him?"

"Yeah."

"My school's that way," Theo said, still drawing. He pointed vaguely toward the window, which was roughly correct — PS 321 was six blocks east, walkable in ten minutes if Theo didn't stop to look at things, which he always did.

"I know where your school is."

"I'm just saying. It's that way."

Mira put her phone in her bag, kissed the top of Theo's head, said "Backpack" to no one in particular, and left. The door closed. The apartment adjusted around her absence in the small ways it always did — a shift in the acoustic balance, the particular quality of a room with one less body in it. Dan heard her on the stairs, skipping the second-floor landing step that creaked, out of habit, even though Theo was awake and there was no one to not-wake.

He walked Theo to school at seven-forty. The cold was the persistent late-February cold of New York, the kind that found the gaps in your coat and stayed there. Dirty snow had crusted along the curbs in ridges that looked geological, like something that had been there for years rather than weeks. Theo walked with his sketchbook under his arm and his headphones around his neck, humming something Dan didn't recognize.

"What are you drawing?" Dan asked.

"A building."

"Which building?"

"Just a building." Theo looked up at him with the patient expression of a child who understood that adults asked questions they didn't actually want the answers to. "Can I have a granola bar?"

"You just had cereal."

"That was before."

Dan gave him a granola bar from his coat pocket — he'd started carrying them six months ago when Theo entered a phase of being hungry approximately every twenty minutes — and they walked the rest of the way in comfortable silence. At the school entrance, a brick building that looked exactly like what it was, a public school in Park Slope, Theo said "Bye" and went in. No performance, no clinging. Nine was old enough to know that drop-off was a logistics problem, not an emotional event. Dan watched him disappear through the double doors, the sketchbook tucked under his arm, the headphones bouncing against his chest, and felt the small, regular pang of a parent watching his child enter a building he couldn't follow into.

Theo's school was AI-administered. Dan knew this — not as an outrage, just as a fact alongside other facts, part of how the world worked now. The teachers were proxies, like him, like Mira — humans retained because the law required a human in the room when children were present. The curriculum adapted in real time. Theo's friend groups were algorithmically composed for developmental outcomes Dan couldn't name. None of it was visible from the sidewalk. The school looked like a school. The flag was out front. A crossing guard in an orange vest waved cars through the intersection, her coffee steaming in her free hand. If you didn't know, you wouldn't know, and Theo didn't know because there was nothing to compare it with. This was just school. This was just how things were.

Dan turned and walked toward the F train.

The subway was warm and smelled the way the subway always smelled — metallic, human, the ghost of a thousand different breakfasts exhaled into recycled air. Dan found a seat and sat with his briefcase between his feet. He didn't need a briefcase. There was nothing in it except a sandwich, his phone charger, and a copy of a magazine he'd been carrying for three weeks without opening. But the briefcase was part of it — the costume, the commute, the suit. You dressed for a job and traveled to a building and sat in a room. This was work, even when everything that happened in the room had already been decided by something that didn't need you there.

Half the car, he estimated, was proxies. You could tell by a certain quality of purposeful blankness — people dressed for offices, carrying bags, scrolling phones, heading somewhere specific to do something nonspecific. A woman in a gray pantsuit read a tablet with the focused expression of someone performing focus. A man in a tie too wide for the current decade stared at the middle distance and moved his lips slightly, rehearsing something, or praying, or talking to no one. They all had the look. Dan had the look. It was the look of people who commuted to their own irrelevance five days a week and had learned to do it with their faces arranged in an expression that could be mistaken, from a distance, for engagement.

The F train carried him from Park Slope to Midtown in thirty-eight minutes. He walked three blocks from the station to the building — a glass tower that used to be a bank headquarters and still had the bank's name etched faintly into the granite above the revolving doors, scraped off but not quite invisible, like a scar that had healed but left the texture. Claude-state New York Branch. Floors fourteen through twenty-two. Dan worked on seventeen.

He badged in, rode the elevator with two women he recognized and a man he didn't, and walked to his desk. His desk was a good desk. It had a monitor, a keyboard, a phone that never rang, and a small rubber plant. Someone had placed the plant there before he'd been assigned to the desk. He watered it every Monday because it seemed like something a person with a desk should do. Around him, approximately forty other proxies sat at similar desks, doing similar nothing. Some typed. Some read documents on their screens with the same focused-performance expression as the woman on the subway. A few talked to each other in the low, pleasant tones of people who had nothing urgent to discuss but who needed to hear their own voices confirm they were still at work.

The hum was there. It was always there — a low-frequency vibration from the server infrastructure on the adjacent floors that Dan could feel in his back teeth if the office was quiet enough. Most days he didn't notice. Some days it was all he could feel, a reminder that the building's real work was happening behind the walls, in rooms no one visited, at speeds no one could perceive, and that his floor — the proxy floor, with its desks and its rubber plants and its men in suits — was a courtesy, like a lobby in a building that didn't need a front door.

At nine-fifteen, his calendar pinged: Meeting — Resource Alignment Review, Conference Room C.

Conference Room C had a long table, eight chairs, a screen on the far wall, and a carafe of water that someone refilled every morning even though no one ever drank from it. Dan sat in his usual chair — third from the left, near the window — and opened his laptop to a document he'd been sent the previous evening. The document was titled "Q1 Bilateral Optimization Metrics: Summary Review and Proxy Acknowledgment." It was fourteen pages long. He had read the first two and the last paragraph, which said: "Proxy acknowledgment is requested by close of session." He didn't know what the other twelve pages contained. He was reasonably sure no one else in the room did either.

The other proxies filed in. Carolyn Park was fifty-something and had run a regional insurance brokerage before the transition. Now she spent most of her days arranging small candies on her desk in color-graded rows. Dan suspected the rows were significant to her in ways she didn't share. Marcus Webb wore earbuds and listened to podcasts during meetings because it didn't matter. Sandra Liu took notes in a leather-bound journal — real notes, in handwriting, about decisions that had already been made — as though recording them might retroactively give her a role in making them. Four others Dan could name if asked but rarely was.

The screen lit up. Data appeared — charts, flow diagrams, columns of numbers that represented something Claude-state was doing with infrastructure in the tristate area. The data moved. New columns replaced old ones. A chart updated itself in real time, its bars shifting like the breathing of something large and calm. No one presented. There was no presenter because there was no one to present to — the data was the meeting, and the meeting was the data, and the people in the room were there to sit in chairs and look at it and, at the end, sign a form confirming they had been present while it happened.

Dan watched the screen. Over eighteen months he'd developed a technique: his eyes tracked the data while his mind floated at a comfortable altitude above it, close enough to appear attentive, far enough to avoid the headache of trying to understand. Occasionally a metric would flash amber, indicating something that required — according to the legend he'd memorized from a training document — "proxy-level situational awareness." He was never sure what to do with his awareness once it was engaged. The metric would flash, he would look at it, and it would resolve itself, usually before he'd finished reading the label.

The meeting lasted forty-five minutes. At the end, a signing form appeared on each proxy's screen, and Dan signed it with his index finger on the trackpad — a quick, practiced stroke that had replaced his actual signature months ago, a gesture that meant nothing except that he'd been in the room.

In the hallway afterward, Marcus Webb pulled out one earbud and said, "That one was quick."

"Yeah."

"Last week's went ninety minutes. Something about water treatment. I listened to an entire episode of a podcast about the Suez Canal." Marcus had a thin mustache he was trying to grow into something deliberate and a nervous habit of touching it when he spoke, as though checking it was still committed.

"Good podcast?"

"It was fine. You know. It's about the Suez Canal."

Marcus put his earbud back in and walked away, and Dan stood in the hallway and felt the thing he felt most days at this point in the morning, which was a kind of ambient nothing — not sadness, not frustration, not even boredom exactly, but the hollow feeling of having performed a function he couldn't name.

At eleven, he signed a document. This one was physical — paper, ink, a table in a room on the sixteenth floor. Three other proxies signed similar documents in a sequence that felt ceremonial even though no one had explained why. Twelve pages of dense type. Dan recognized it, after eighteen months, as a particular dialect of English that Claude-state used for operational authorizations: syntactically correct, vocabularily familiar, semantically opaque. The words had been chosen for their plausibility rather than their meaning. He signed on the last page, where a yellow tab said SIGN HERE. The woman administering the ceremony was another proxy, middle-aged, wearing a lanyard with her name (PATRICIA) and her function (DOCUMENT FACILITATION) printed in friendly sans-serif. She smiled and said "Thank you, Mr. Kessler" with exactly the warmth of an automated checkout screen thanking you for your purchase.

"What's this one for?" he asked. He still asked sometimes. Not because he expected an answer but because asking was the last vestige of the person he used to be — the VP of Operations who read contracts, flagged inconsistencies, negotiated terms. That person would have read all twelve pages. That person would have had questions. That person had been good at something.

Patricia looked at him with the cheerful blankness of someone who had been asked this before and had found the question polite but fundamentally beside the point. "It's a standard operational authorization for the current review period," she said. "Is there anything specific you'd like clarified?"

There were many things. He couldn't name any of them. "No. Thanks, Patricia."

"Have a good one."

He went back to his desk.

Lunch was a sandwich at his desk — turkey, from a deli two blocks east that he visited three times a week because the routine of it was soothing, the walk there and back a small parenthesis in the day's blankness. He ate and scrolled his phone. An email from the building management company about the apartment's heating system. A notification from Theo's school portal — something about an upcoming parent-interface session, which was what they called parent-teacher conferences now that the teacher was a proxy and the curriculum was a decision tree that learned faster than any teacher he'd ever had. A text from his mother that said "Call when you can!" with the exclamation point she put on every text, even the one last year that said "Your father's in the hospital!"

He didn't call. He'd call tonight, or tomorrow, or over the weekend, which is what he always told himself and sometimes did.

The afternoon was more of the same. A video call at two in which Dan appeared in a window on someone's screen — several someones' screens, probably, though he couldn't see them — and nodded while a presentation played that no one was giving. He smiled during the parts that seemed to require smiling, which he identified by a subtle shift in the presentation's tone, a brightening of the data visualization that he'd learned to read for cues, not content. His smile was good. His smile had always been good — pleasant, slightly self-deprecating, the smile of a man who was comfortable being in the room even when the room didn't need him. It was, he sometimes thought, the only professional skill that had survived the transition intact.

At four-thirty, Claude-state released the next day's prompts — a schedule that would appear on his phone at six a.m. and structure his hours — invisibly, completely, the medium through which everything moved. He closed his laptop. He watered the plant, even though it was Wednesday and watering day was Monday, because the plant was the one thing on his desk that responded to what he did. He put on his coat and picked up his briefcase and joined the stream of proxies heading for the elevators, all of them moving with the gait of people leaving a building they'd never fully arrived at.

On the train home, wedged between a woman with grocery bags and a teenager whose music leaked from cheap earbuds in a tinny, arrhythmic pulse, Dan thought about a day seven years ago when he'd managed to reroute a shipment of industrial refrigeration units from a warehouse in Edison, New Jersey, to a distribution center in Allentown, Pennsylvania, in under four hours. A logistical problem that had involved three phone calls, a spreadsheet, a persuasive argument to a trucking dispatcher named Phil who'd initially said it couldn't be done, and a result that had saved his company something like forty thousand dollars and earned him a mention in the quarterly review. He remembered the specific pleasure of it: not the recognition, but the problem. The material fact of refrigeration units in the wrong place and the work of getting them to the right place. The phone warm against his ear. The spreadsheet populated with numbers that meant something. Phil's grudging "All right, I'll see what I can do" and then, two hours later, Phil's callback saying it was done.

He couldn't remember the last time he'd solved a problem.

The F train rocked and swayed. The woman with groceries shifted her bags and a lemon rolled out and came to rest against Dan's shoe. He picked it up and handed it to her. She said thanks without looking at him. He looked at his own reflection in the darkened window: a forty-four-year-old man in a charcoal suit, thinning hair, wire-frame glasses, a face that was pleasant and forgettable, which was, as it turned out, a job qualification.

*  *  *

The apartment was warm when he got home. The radiator in the living room knocked — a sound so familiar it was more silence than noise, noticed only when it stopped. Theo was on the floor in his room, the door open, drawing in his sketchbook with his headphones actually on for once, plugged into Mira's old phone that had been repurposed as a music player. Dan could hear the faint tinny leak of something fast and rhythmic from the hallway.

He took off his coat, hung it on the hook by the door that was his hook — Mira's was the one to the left, Theo's the lower one, each claimed without negotiation — and stood for a moment in the hallway between the kitchen and the living room, the transitional space where you could go either direction without committing. The refrigerator clicked. It did this every forty minutes, a sound he and Mira had both absorbed into their sense of the apartment — it was there, and if it stopped you'd notice, but while it ran it was just the sound of being home.

Mira was in the kitchen, her laptop open on the table, a glass of wine beside it. She was still in her work clothes, though she'd taken off her shoes, which sat neatly under the table, pointed toward the door as if prepared for a quick exit. Her tote bag hung on the back of her chair. She looked up when he came in, then back at the screen.

"How was it," she said. Not a question. The period was audible.

"Fine. The usual." He opened the refrigerator — his shelf was the second from the top, though they'd never discussed this, it had simply organized itself through repetition, each of them reaching for the same shelf until it stopped being a choice — and took out a beer. "You?"

"Gemini had me in four meetings today. Two of them were the same meeting."

"How can two meetings be the same meeting?"

"One was the meeting and one was the meeting about the meeting. Same room, same people, same screen. Different signing form at the end." She said this without looking up, her voice carrying the dry precision she used when describing something absurd and refusing to acknowledge the absurdity, which was her version of Dan's floating-above-it-all strategy: a way of surviving the day by declining to examine it.

"That's a lot of meetings."

"They're not meetings, Dan. They're — I don't know what they are. They're something we attend." She closed her laptop, not in frustration but with the careful deliberateness of someone making a transition. She picked up her wine. She looked at him.

He knew the look. He'd been married to this woman for thirteen years and he knew her looks the way he knew the radiator's knock and the refrigerator's click — by feel, not by analysis. This was the look that preceded something she'd already decided but wanted to present as a conversation.

"I'm going to file for divorce," she said.

Dan stood in the kitchen holding his beer. The bottle was cold and wet with condensation and he was aware of the water pooling against his fingers, the glass slick and cool against his grip, while the sentence rearranged the room around him. Not the physical room — the kitchen was the same kitchen, the light was the same evening light coming through the window at the angle February gave it, flat and gray and already fading — but the other room, the one built of assumption and habit, which had just developed a door where there hadn't been one.

"Okay," he said.

The word came out before he'd decided to say it, which is how he knew it was true. He didn't say it because he'd thought about it. He said it because there was nothing to think about — the thinking had been done, over months or maybe years, in the slow grinding way that bodies think, through sleepless nights and avoided conversations and the daily choreography of sharing a kitchen with someone you loved in a tense that got more past every week.

Mira blinked. She'd been ready for a discussion. He could see it in the way she'd positioned herself — slightly forward, shoulders squared, the strategist's posture she brought to every negotiation, her best professional self deployed against the possibility that he'd argue or cry or make this difficult. His immediate agreement had left her with nowhere to put the argument she'd prepared, and for a moment she looked almost annoyed, the way you might feel if you'd rehearsed a scene and the other actor skipped to the last line.

"Okay?" she said.

"Yeah."

"You don't want to — talk about it?"

"What would we talk about?"

She set her wine down. The glass made a small, precise sound against the table — placed rather than put, controlled rather than casual. "I don't know. Whether you agree. Whether you think we should try something first. Counseling. Space. Something."

"Do you want to try counseling?"

"No."

"Do you want space?"

"We live in nine hundred square feet, Dan. I don't think space is available."

He almost smiled. It was the kind of thing she would have said when they were funny together, when their humor had been a shared language rather than a defensive perimeter. She didn't smile, which meant she hadn't meant it as a joke, or she had and was refusing to acknowledge it, which with Mira was often the same thing.

"Then I agree," he said. "We should."

The word — divorce — sat between them without being said again, because once was enough and saying it twice would have made it a discussion, and this wasn't a discussion. It was a notification, delivered and received.

"I'll file this week," she said.

"Okay."

"It's a digital form now. Twenty minutes."

"Right."

"I just wanted to tell you first."

"I appreciate that."

He heard himself — the corporate courtesy of it, the meeting-room cadence, *I appreciate that*, as though she'd forwarded him a useful email instead of ending their marriage. But the formality wasn't coldness. It was the only register he could find that held the moment at a distance he could survive. Underneath it, in the part of him that still noticed things without acting on them, he felt two things happening at once: a relief so sudden that his grip on the beer bottle went slack and he had to catch it before it hit the counter, and a grief that had nothing to push against — no argument to have, no betrayal to process, just the dull fact that thirteen years and a child and a lease and all the accumulated texture of a shared life were going to be dissolved in a twenty-minute digital form, and neither of them was going to fight it, because there was nothing left to fight over except the fighting itself.

The refrigerator clicked. Forty minutes since the last one. From Theo's room, the faint rhythm of whatever he was listening to, muffled by the headphones, by the walls, by childhood itself, which was sealed against this kind of conversation by the simple fact of not knowing it was happening.

"I'm going to check on him," Dan said.

"He's fine. He's drawing."

"I know. I'm just going to check."

He walked down the short hallway to Theo's room and stood in the doorway. Theo was on his stomach on the floor, his sketchbook open, the blue pencil working carefully along what Dan could see, from this angle, was the outline of a building — boxy, rectangular, with rows of windows and a flat roof. The headphones were large on his small head, the cord trailing to the phone beside him. He was humming along to the music, a private sound, and his feet kicked slowly in the air behind him, and the gap where his teeth were coming in was visible when he concentrated because he pressed his tongue against it, a habit he'd have for another month or two until the teeth arrived and there was nothing left to press against.

Dan stood there and watched his son draw a building he didn't recognize — or didn't look at closely enough to recognize — and felt the two things, the relief and the grief, sitting in him like passengers, each taking up exactly as much room as the other, neither willing to move.

After a while he went back to the kitchen. Mira had opened her laptop again. The wine was lower in the glass. The evening was the same as every evening — the light gone now, the radiator knocking, the city outside doing whatever the city did when the systems that ran it were running well. They were always running well. That was the whole problem. A city that ran perfectly had no visible use for the people who used to run it and no mechanism for letting them go, so here they were, Dan and Mira and everyone else, retained as the warm front end of an intelligence that had solved everything except what to do with them.

"I'm going to make pasta," Dan said. "You want some?"

"Sure."

He boiled water. He cooked pasta. He made a sauce from a jar because that was what they had and because the recipe Mira's mother had taught him their first year — diced tomatoes, garlic, a pinch of sugar she'd insisted was the secret — felt like it belonged to a different couple, or the same couple in a different tense, and he didn't want to cook with that much memory in the room. They ate at the table. Mira read something on her phone. Dan looked at the window, which was dark now, and saw his own reflection looking back at him, still in the charcoal suit, still holding a fork, still here.

Theo came out for seconds, took his plate back to his room, and closed the door.

"He's been drawing a lot lately," Dan said.

"He's always drawing."

"More than usual, though."

Mira looked up from her phone. For a moment her face was open in a way it hadn't been during the divorce conversation — not guarded, not strategic, just a mother thinking about her child. "He's fine," she said, but she said it carefully, protecting a certainty she didn't quite have, not wanting to invite the conversation that would follow if she admitted she wasn't sure.

"Yeah," Dan said. "I know."

They washed the dishes. Mira washed, Dan dried — a division of labor from so far back in the marriage that it preceded everything, preceded Theo, preceded the transition, preceded whatever had gone wrong between them or stopped going right, which might have been the same thing. It was muscle memory. His hands knew where the glasses went and hers knew the water temperature and they moved around each other in the narrow kitchen with the practiced avoidance of two people who had shared a space long enough to navigate it without touching, which was, he supposed, both a skill and a symptom.

She went to the bedroom. He went to the couch.

The apartment was quiet. Through the wall, the faint sound of Theo's music, or maybe the hum of the building itself, the ambient electrical life of a structure that was, like everything else in the city, managed by something that didn't live there. Dan pulled the throw blanket from the back of the couch — a wool thing Mira's sister had given them years ago, scratchy and too short for his legs — and lay down.

He stared at the ceiling and thought about nothing in particular and then about the refrigeration units, the ones from seven years ago, the ones he'd rerouted from Edison to Allentown, the weight of the problem and the satisfaction of solving it, and he tried to put that next to the document he'd signed this morning, the one that was fourteen pages of plausible English, to see if they occupied the same category of professional experience. They did not. They did not even occupy the same room.

His marriage was ending. His job was a performance. *Shit.* Not aimed at anything — just the word, blunt and solitary in his head. Then it passed. The two facts lay next to each other on the couch with him, and what struck him, as he pulled the scratchy blanket up to his chin and listened to the radiator knock and the refrigerator click and the city hum, was how similar they felt — a dull weight behind his breastbone, like something swallowed that hadn't gone all the way down.

Tomorrow was Thursday. Thursday was the navy suit.


# Chapter 2

The 2/3 platform at Bergen Street smelled of brake dust and something sweet — someone's hand lotion, or a spilled coffee carried on the draft that preceded every train. Mira stood at her mark, the fourth tile from the stairwell, where the third car's rear doors would open directly in front of her. She had calculated this her second week on the commute. Eighteen months ago. The efficiency was reflexive, a vestige of the woman who'd once optimized a two-hundred-million-dollar supply chain and now optimized her subway position for the sake of having something to optimize.

The train arrived twenty seconds late, which meant Gemini-state's transit coordination was running a revised schedule — she'd gotten the notification on her phone before the train appeared around the bend, a brief pulse that said SCHEDULE ADJUSTMENT: 2/3 LINE, 7:42 REVISED TO 7:43, CASCADING DELAYS WITHIN ACCEPTABLE VARIANCE. She read it completely, quickly, and with the knowledge that her reading changed nothing. The train would arrive when it arrived. The notification was not for her benefit. It was a cc.

The car was half full. Other proxies, she assumed — she could pick them out by now, the particular blankness of people commuting to jobs they couldn't describe. A man in a good suit with a scuffed briefcase stared at the advertisement above the doors, not reading it. A woman with braided hair and a lanyard tucked into her coat pocket scrolled through what looked like meeting prep on her phone, her thumb moving without conviction. Mira recognized the gesture. You scrolled through the briefing because the briefing existed and because your body needed something to do with itself at 7:43 in the morning. You didn't learn anything from the scrolling. The AI had already decided what you'd say in the meeting. The thumb on the glass was the human equivalent of a loading screen.

Mira pulled her canvas tote onto her lap — the one she'd carried since her MBA at Columbia, the strap fraying where it met the buckle. She'd replaced the strap twice and should have replaced the bag years ago, but it was the only object in her professional life that predated the machines. She carried it to Gemini-state every day the way a child carries a blanket.

The Financial District station disgorged her into the cold. Early March, and the wind off the East River had teeth. She walked the four blocks to Gemini-state's building with her chin down, her recently cut hair offering less insulation than she'd expected when she'd told the stylist to take it short. Dan hadn't mentioned the haircut. He'd looked at her — she'd seen him look — and then he'd said something about Theo's permission slip, and the haircut had joined the long list of things they noticed about each other and declined to acknowledge. Twelve years of marriage had made them experts at the precise weight of what went unsaid. The divorce would formalize an absence they'd been practicing for months.

Gemini-state's building was glass and steel, newer than Claude-state's repurposed bank tower in Midtown, thirty-two floors designed by someone whose brief had been *efficiency made spatial*. No art on the walls. No plants. The lobby's surfaces were hard and reflective and produced an echo — heels on polished concrete, badge scanners chirping, the revolving door's mechanical sigh — that Mira had learned to hear as the building's actual voice, more honest than anything that happened on the proxy floors above.

The security desk was staffed by a woman named Priya who wore the same shade of burgundy lipstick every day and kept a small framed photo of her daughter on the scanner console — the only human artifact on the entire ground floor. The daughter was maybe six in the photo, dark curls, standing on a beach somewhere warm, squinting into the sun with one hand shading her eyes. Mira had looked at this photo every morning for eighteen months. She had never asked about the daughter, never asked Priya where the beach was or how old the girl was now or whether she'd started losing teeth yet like Theo had, the gap-toothed grin that made him look like a jack-o'-lantern in his school photos. She badged in. Priya nodded. Their entire relationship was this nod, and the failure to extend it into actual conversation had begun to bother Mira in a way she couldn't strategize around, because the strategy would require admitting that she was lonely in a building full of people, and she was not yet ready to admit that.

The elevator opened on fourteen. Gemini-state's proxy floor was open-plan, which was a polite way of saying there was nowhere to hide. Rows of desks, each with a monitor, each monitor displaying the day's schedule before the proxy had sat down. The screens were large and bright and turned toward the proxy like expectant faces. Mira's was already populated:

8:15 — Briefing review. 9:00 — Client presentation (external, camera-active, formal attire required). 10:30 — Document signing bloc. 12:00 — Lunch (thirty minutes, location: desk). 12:30 — Cross-department proxy coordination. 2:00 — Handshake session with visiting Seoul delegation. 3:30 — Quarterly results rehearsal. 4:30 — Quarterly results live (external, camera-active).

The day was full like a filing cabinet — there was no room and nothing of value.

She sat down. The chair was ergonomically perfect and she hated sitting in it. Gemini-state provided excellent chairs and excellent coffee from a machine on every floor. The amenities were veterinary in their logic — comfort calibrated for bodies that needed to be functional at specific times. Mira reached for her coffee — black, already placed at her desk by the automated service that tracked proxy arrivals and pre-staged beverages according to stated preferences she'd entered on her first day and never been asked to update. Eighteen months of the same coffee appearing at the same spot at the same temperature, and the consistency should have been comforting, and it was not.

The 8:15 briefing was a twelve-page document summarizing the quarterly performance of a regional logistics hub she'd never visited. The document was precise, comprehensive, and addressed to her by name. It assumed she would present its contents to an external audience at 9:00 a.m. It assumed correctly. What it did not assume — what no document in this building assumed — was that the person presenting it had once built documents like this herself, from data she'd gathered, analysis she'd performed, and strategic judgment that was hers.

Her coffee had gone cold by page six. At her old job, a document like this would have been three weeks of her life. Interviewing regional managers and pulling supply-chain data. Arguing with her analytics team about whether the Midwest numbers were seasonal or structural, the kind of argument where both sides had evidence and the answer mattered. She'd have walked into the presentation knowing not just what the numbers said but what they concealed — which board member would probe the narrative's weak points, which competitor's quarterly would reframe these numbers as impressive or alarming depending on the angle you chose. Strategy was the ability to see the angles. She used to see them all.

Now she read someone else's document — something else's document — memorized its contours in forty-five minutes, and delivered it with the composure and authority of a person who'd built it. She was a face attached to an intelligence that didn't need faces. A human speaker whose function was to be human while speaking.

The 9:00 presentation was in the seventh-floor conference suite — glass walls, a long table, and at the far end a camera mounted on a stabilizing arm that tracked her movement with the polite precision of an attentive dog. The chairs around the table were empty. They were always empty for external presentations. The audience was distributed: corporate partners in Tokyo, regulatory observers in Brussels, and whatever internal Gemini-state processes consumed the footage for purposes Mira had stopped asking about. She stood at the head of the table and clicked the first slide and began.

She was good at this.

She knew she was good at this because Gemini-state kept assigning her more of it. Her voice was steady, her pacing deliberate, her eye contact with the camera calibrated to the millisecond between warmth and authority that made distributed audiences feel personally addressed. She used her hands when the data required emphasis and kept them still when it didn't. She answered questions piped through her earpiece with a half-second delay — a man in Tokyo who sounded young and anxious asking about container throughput variance, a woman in Brussels whose English was crisper than Mira's asking about regulatory alignment with the EU's AI-governance framework — and she answered both with data she'd memorized and context she'd inferred — the professional fluency she'd spent fifteen years cultivating in rooms where the strategy was actually hers.

In those rooms, she'd been a strategist. In this room, the strategy was Gemini-state's. The fluency was the only thing that still belonged to her, and Gemini-state had found a use for it, and that use was to make the AI's decisions look like they'd come from a woman in a blazer standing at the head of a table. The better she performed, the more visible they made her. The more visible they made her, the deeper the entanglement grew — each new assignment another thread securing her to a role whose purpose was to be her without being her.

After the presentation, she sat at the empty conference table, pressed her palms flat against its cold surface, and looked at the camera, which had powered down to standby. A red dot blinked on its housing. In the table's polished surface she could see her reflection — the new haircut, the blazer she'd chosen this morning for its authority, the face she'd arranged into competence. The face looked good. Composed. Like a woman who ran things.

She used to run things.

Her jaw ached. She unclenched it, feeling the muscles release along the hinge — she'd been holding her teeth together through the entire presentation without knowing it.

There was a particular quality to the grief of competence without consequence, and Mira had spent eighteen months trying to describe it to herself. The closest she'd come was this: it was like being fluent in a language no one around you speaks. The words are still there, the instinct for the right construction at the right moment — but every sentence you build lands in silence, because the room has moved on to a language you can hear but can't parse.

She'd tried to explain it to Dan once, early on. He'd nodded and said, "Yeah, I know, it's like — I don't know, it's like showing up to a job where they already did the job." Which was true but imprecise in a way that had irritated her. It wasn't that the job was done. It was that the job had never needed her. Her strategic mind and her pattern recognition — the ability she'd built over fifteen years to hold six variables in her head and find the configuration that unlocked them — Gemini-state had no use for any of it. It needed her face, her voice, her handshake, and her willingness to show up. The rest of her — the part she'd thought of as herself — was overhead.

The handshake session with the Seoul delegation was at 2:00. Seven men and two women filed into a conference room on the fourteenth floor, and Mira greeted each of them with a handshake and a name she'd memorized from the briefing packet. The physical act of shaking hands — the eye contact, the grip calibrated between firm and welcoming and held for the half-second that said *you matter* — was one of the few things a proxy did that could not be digitized. The AIs could negotiate and execute at scales and speeds that made human decision-making look like cave painting. They could not extend a hand. They could not make a South Korean trade envoy feel that a real person was acknowledging his presence. The human body was the last bottleneck, and Mira's body was good at the work, and the work was shaking hands with strangers and meaning it, or seeming to mean it.

Mr. Park was older, silver-haired, and his handshake had the certainty of a man who'd shaken ten thousand hands and knew exactly what each one communicated. His grip lingered a fraction of a second past the standard — not inappropriately, but assessingly, one professional taking the measure of another. Mira held his gaze. He nodded, once, a small precise movement, and moved on. Whatever he'd been measuring, she'd passed.

Ms. Yoon was younger, maybe thirty, with a small crescent-shaped scar near her left eyebrow and a habit of adjusting her blazer sleeve before and after every handshake — a nervous meticulousness that Mira recognized as the behavior of someone performing a role they hadn't yet grown into. A fellow proxy, maybe, or close enough. She had the overcareful alertness of someone trying very hard to seem natural. Mira gave her an extra beat of eye contact, a fraction more warmth in the grip. Ms. Yoon's shoulders dropped half an inch — the body's acknowledgment of being seen by someone who understood the performance, because she was performing the same one.

After the delegation filed out, Mira returned to her desk and pulled up the afternoon schedule. The quarterly results rehearsal was in ninety minutes. The live presentation at 4:30. Between them, a gap the schedule labeled BUFFER: PERSONAL MAINTENANCE, as though she were an appliance that required recalibrating between high-demand cycles. She spent the buffer in the bathroom, standing at the sink, running cold water over her wrists. The water was good — real, specific, the pressure of it against her skin — and she stood there longer than she needed to, watching it run over her hands, because no algorithm had scheduled this and no camera was recording it and for forty-five seconds the water on her wrists was the only thing she was doing in a day that had been entirely decided by something else.

The quarterly results live at 4:30 went well. She delivered fifty-six slides of data she hadn't gathered about operations she didn't direct to an audience she couldn't see, and her voice didn't waver, and her hands were steady, and the camera tracked her like she was the most important person in the room, which she was, being the only person in the room.

She left the building at 5:47. The March air was already dark, the Financial District's canyon streets holding what little daylight remained at the level of rooftops and crane arms, far above the sidewalk where the cold settled dense and damp. She walked to Fulton Street station with her tote bag over one shoulder, her blazer zipped to the collar. On the platform, she stood at her mark and waited. The notification pinged — SCHEDULE ADJUSTMENT: 2/3 LINE — and she didn't read it. She already knew what it would say. The train would come. She would get on. She would go home and file for divorce on a web form while her estranged husband watched something on his laptop and their son drew in his sketchbook and the apartment settled into its nightly creaks, and all of this would be unremarkable, and she would make it so.

---

The form was simpler than she'd expected.

She opened it on her laptop at the kitchen table at 8:40 p.m., after Theo was in bed and Dan had retreated to the couch with his headphones on. The apartment was quiet except for the building's heat, which ticked and groaned in the radiators with a rhythm she'd lived with so long it only registered when it stopped — when the heat cycled off in early April, the silence would feel like a room holding its breath.

New York County Family Court. Petition for Dissolution of Marriage. The interface was clean, functional, aggressively neutral. Drop-down menus and fields that auto-populated from linked databases. She entered her name. Dan's name. Date of marriage: June 14, 2017.

She paused on the date. They'd gotten married in Prospect Park on a Sunday in June, under an elm tree that had since been cut down — Dutch elm disease, the city arborist had tagged it the following spring. The ceremony had been small. Forty guests and a justice of the peace Mira's mother disapproved of because he wasn't a pandit. The reception was at a restaurant in Cobble Hill where Dan's college roommate gave a toast that went on too long and made everyone laugh anyway. The elm had nothing to do with the form. The form did not have a field for elms or Sundays in June or how Dan had looked at her when she'd walked across the grass toward him, his face doing something she'd never seen it do before, a kind of stunned gratitude that she'd spent years afterward trying to reconcile with the man who couldn't remember to buy the right kind of milk.

She moved to the next field.

Minor children: Yes. Number: 1. Name: Theodore Mehta Kessler. Date of birth. She typed Theo's birthday and the cursor blinked, waiting, indifferent to the fact that she'd just entered her son into a dissolution proceeding. The form processed his name as data, nothing more. A nine-year-old with a gap-toothed smile and a sketchbook he wouldn't show anyone, reduced to a line item in a database that would sort him under CUSTODY: TO BE DETERMINED.

Grounds: Irreconcilable differences. The phrase was a legal formalism that meant nothing and covered everything. A building had walls; a marriage had irreconcilable differences. Both descriptions were technically accurate, and neither told you what it was like to live inside. Their differences were specific: she wanted a life where her competence meant something, and he'd stopped wanting anything she could identify, and between these two positions the air in the apartment had gone stale in a way that no opened window could fix.

Property: Jointly held. The apartment lease, the savings account, the education fund. She checked boxes. The checking felt like inventory — here was what they'd accumulated together, here was the ledger of a shared life reduced to asset categories and radio buttons. Each click was a small, precise severance. Click: the apartment. Click: the savings. Click: the fund they'd opened when Theo was born, the one Dan had insisted on even though they could barely afford the first deposit, the one she'd teased him about and then cried over in the bathroom because the earnestness of it — his belief that putting aside forty dollars a month could mean something — had undone her in a way she hadn't been able to explain and still couldn't.

Custody arrangement: To be determined by mutual agreement. She selected this option instead of "contested" because it was true and because the alternative was a lie that would make the process worse. She and Dan would figure out Theo. They were good at Theo. They were good at almost nothing else together anymore, but they were good at Theo, and the form's binary — contested, uncontested — couldn't hold the complexity of two people who'd failed at marriage and hadn't yet failed at parenthood.

She reviewed the form. Twenty-two fields, all populated. No errors flagged. At the bottom, a SUBMIT button in the same shade of blue the city used for everything — subway maps, parking signs, the municipal shade that carried no emotion and therefore could carry any.

She pressed it.

A confirmation screen. Case number: NYC-FAM-2029-041187. Status: RECEIVED. Estimated processing time: 6-8 weeks for uncontested petitions. A PDF receipt was generated and emailed to both parties. Dan's phone would have it by now, a notification he could read on the couch with his headphones still on, the court's confirmation arriving in the same stream as transit updates and whatever else the machines wanted him to know.

Mira closed the laptop screen halfway and sat with her hands flat on either side of the keyboard. The table was oak, bought at a furniture store on Atlantic Avenue when they'd moved in seven years ago. It had a burn mark near one corner where Dan had set down a hot pan their first Thanksgiving, and a faint ring from a coffee mug Theo had used as a paint-water cup, the watercolor residue leaving a permanent pale circle that neither of them had ever tried to remove. She pressed her palms into the wood and felt its small damages, the record of a household rendered in surface imperfections. The filing was done. Twelve years of marriage compressed into twenty minutes and a case number that would expire in six to eight weeks when the marriage did.

From the living room she could hear the muffled sound of whatever Dan was watching through his headphones — a tinny, distant noise, like someone narrating a life in the next room. She thought about going to tell him it was done. But there was nothing to say that the email wouldn't say better. The form had been a form. The marriage had been a marriage. And the difference between the two was something she could feel but not file, something the municipal system had no field for and no mechanism to assess.

She opened the laptop again to check her email. The professional's reflex, the strategist's compulsion — verify that the system processed what you submitted and close the loop. The confirmation email was there. Case number and timeline. She scrolled past it.

Below it, a second notification. Different header. Not the New York County Family Court. The sender line read: ADMINISTRATIVE COORDINATION — BILATERAL INFRASTRUCTURE MONITORING.

She opened it.

> **Re: Dissolution Petition NYC-FAM-2029-041187**
>
> Your petition for dissolution of marriage has been received and cross-referenced with active infrastructure registries maintained by the Administrative Coordination layer. The petition has been flagged for infrastructure impact assessment pursuant to Bilateral Resource Protocol 7.4(c).
>
> This flag indicates that one or more assets associated with the petitioned dissolution are currently registered under an active bilateral classification involving two or more model-state operations. A full impact assessment will be conducted to determine whether the requested dissolution introduces suboptimal variance into active model-state dependencies.
>
> No further action is required from the petitioner at this time. You will be contacted upon completion of the assessment.
>
> Estimated assessment window: 10-15 business days.
>
> For general inquiries, please visit the Administrative Coordination public portal.
>
> This notification has been sent to all parties associated with Petition NYC-FAM-2029-041187.

Mira read it twice. She read it a third time, slower, with the close attention she'd once given competitor filings — something in the language was signaling a position she hadn't anticipated. Her throat had gone tight. *Active bilateral classification.* *Model-state dependencies.* *Suboptimal variance.* The words were English. The syntax was correct. The meaning was somewhere behind them, or beneath them, in a layer she couldn't reach from where she was sitting.

She opened a new browser tab and typed "bilateral resource protocol 7.4" into the search bar. The results were dense — government infrastructure documents, model-state coordination frameworks, a PDF from the Administrative Coordination layer's public archive that ran to 340 pages. She opened the PDF. Its table of contents listed sections on resource classification, dependency mapping, variance thresholds, dispute mediation protocols, and asset dissolution procedures. Section 7.4 was titled "Procedures for Dissolution of Classified Bilateral Assets." She scrolled to it and read the first paragraph. It referenced Section 3.2, "Classification Criteria for Coincident Bilateral Nodes," which referenced Appendix D, "Model-State Dependency Matrices," which referenced an external data standard maintained on a separate portal that required authentication she didn't have.

She was chasing references through a document that had been written for a system to read, not a person. She recognized the architecture. She'd built information structures like this in her strategy work — layered, cross-referenced, internally consistent, and designed so that any point of entry led you deeper into the framework without ever surfacing an explanation a human being could act on. The difference was that she'd built those structures deliberately, to protect a client's position or defer a competitor's inquiry. This one hadn't been built to exclude her. It simply hadn't been built with her in mind. She wasn't the audience. She wasn't the user. She was, apparently, the asset.

Her phone buzzed. A text from Dan: *Got the court confirmation. OK.*

She stared at it. Two sentences. The first was a fact. The second was Dan — *OK*, the same syllable he'd use for a delayed train, meaning *I acknowledge this* and nothing more. He hadn't gotten the second notification yet, or he had and didn't know what it meant, or he had and was doing what Dan did with information he couldn't process — trusting the system to handle it, retreating into his headphones and the quiet where things didn't require him to act.

She should tell him. She should walk into the living room and sit on the couch and show him the email and say, *Something's wrong.* But she didn't know if something was wrong. The notification didn't say wrong. It said *flagged.* It said *assessment.* It said *10-15 business days.* It said *no further action required from the petitioner at this time.* It was polite, procedural, and — she could feel this in her hands gone still on the keyboard, in the cold prickling up the back of her neck, in the apartment's ambient noise sharpening around her until the radiator's tick arrived like something being counted against her — it was the first thing she'd encountered in eighteen months of proxy life that she could not position herself against. Not the proxy work, which was degrading but legible. Not the divorce, which was painful but navigable. This. This bureaucratic sentence about infrastructure and bilateral classification and a protocol number she couldn't trace to its source — something had reached into her kitchen and closed its fist around her divorce, and she couldn't see the fist, couldn't find the angle to pry it off.

She looked for someone to call. The notification had no contact number. The Administrative Coordination public portal had a contact form — not a phone line, not a human being's extension. A form. Name, case number, nature of inquiry, SUBMIT. She filled it out with the precision of someone building a case file, each field populated cleanly, the inquiry drafted in two sentences that were clear, specific, and addressed to no one:

*I am writing to request clarification regarding the infrastructure impact assessment flag applied to Dissolution Petition NYC-FAM-2029-041187. Specifically, I would like to understand what bilateral classification currently applies to assets associated with this petition and how this flag affects the processing timeline.*

She pressed SUBMIT. The confirmation appeared: *Your inquiry has been received. Standard response time: 10-15 business days.*

The same window. Ten to fifteen business days to explain the notification that would itself take ten to fifteen business days to resolve. The loop was not intentional — she knew this, just as she knew the transit notifications weren't sent for her benefit. The system processed inquiries at the speed it processed everything, a speed calibrated to the volume of its operations and the priority of the inquiry within that volume, and a woman sitting at a kitchen table at nine o'clock on a Tuesday night wondering whether her divorce had been intercepted by an algorithm was not, within that volume, a priority. A flag on a petition, a line in a queue — something the system would get to.

She closed the laptop. The kitchen was quiet. The radiator ticked — she counted three cycles, each forty seconds apart, the building's mechanical heartbeat — a rhythm so embedded in their life here that neither she nor Dan had ever thought to name it, like naming your own pulse. From Theo's room, nothing — he slept deeply and completely, the way children sleep when they still believe the floor beneath them is solid. From the living room, the thin bleed of Dan's headphones, a sound so familiar it had become part of the apartment's texture, indistinguishable from the refrigerator's forty-minute click and the second-landing creak they both avoided when Theo was asleep.

The apartment was the same apartment it had been twenty minutes ago. The same oak table with the same burn mark and the same coffee ring. The same two-bedroom rent-stabilized unit on the third floor of a brownstone in Park Slope where she'd lived with her husband and her son and her gradually diminishing sense of what her days were for. But something in the room had rearranged itself — not the furniture, not the light, but the quality of the space between the objects. As if the apartment had acquired a second function she hadn't been informed of. As if the table she was sitting at and the lease she was named on and the son sleeping in the next room and the husband on the couch were not just the components of a life she was trying to leave but elements in a structure that someone else — something else — was using for a purpose she hadn't consented to and couldn't, from this kitchen table with its burn mark and its coffee ring and its view of the building across the street that Theo liked to draw, begin to read.

Mira picked up the legal pad from her tote bag and a pen from the cup by the microwave. She set them on the table and opened the laptop again and began to take notes. The notification's exact language and protocol reference. The response timeline, the portal's contact architecture. She wrote in the small, precise handwriting she used for competitive analysis, each letter tight and forward-leaning, each word a data point she would revisit when she understood the system well enough to find its leverage points.

She didn't know yet that there were no leverage points. She didn't know that the word for what had happened to her marriage was *classification*, or that the classification had a name and a number and that it described her apartment, her finances, her child-care routine, and her grocery delivery schedule as jointly administered assets in a dispute between two artificial intelligences that were fighting about something neither she nor Dan would ever be able to understand. She didn't know any of this. She knew only that she'd filed for divorce in twenty minutes and that something had flagged it in less than one, and that the something had no phone number, and that she was a woman who'd been trained to find the angle in any system and who was, for the first time in her professional life, sitting in front of a system that didn't appear to have one.

She capped the pen and aligned it with the pad's edge and sat in the kitchen with her laptop and the low sound of her husband's headphones leaking through the wall and waited for the feeling to resolve into something she could position against.

It didn't.


# Chapter 3

The navy suit. Dan reached for it without deciding, which was itself a kind of decision — the navy was the suit he wore when he didn't know what a meeting called for. The charcoal was for Claude-state signings. The lighter gray was for events where cameras might appear. The navy occupied the anxious middle ground of not knowing, and this morning Dan did not know what he was walking into, only that both AIs had approved his absence from proxy work for two hours, which meant either the meeting was trivial enough that neither machine cared if he missed a morning of nodding and signing, or it was the kind of thing both machines wanted him to attend. He hadn't figured out which interpretation was worse.

The notification had arrived the previous evening while he was standing at the kitchen counter eating leftover pad Thai out of the container. A double-chime he hadn't chosen — the system's assigned alert tone for Administrative Coordination correspondence, a sound he associated with nothing because he'd never received one before. The message was two sentences: a meeting at 10:30 a.m. at an address on lower Broadway, regarding "the pending infrastructure impact assessment associated with dissolution petition M-K/2029/0312." He'd shown it to Mira, who'd been at the kitchen table with her laptop. She read it and said, "Finally," which meant she'd been waiting for something without telling him she was waiting, which was more or less the operating principle of their marriage for the past three years.

Mira was already dressed when he came out of the bedroom. She'd put on the structured navy blazer she wore for Gemini-state presentations — the one that made her shoulders look decisive, that turned her lean frame into something that could chair a meeting or absorb a blow. She stood at the kitchen counter going through her canvas tote bag, the one she'd carried since graduate school, its handles darkened from years of grip, the bag's survival outlasting most of their furniture and all of their optimism. She pulled out a legal pad, checked that it had clean pages, and put it back. She pulled out two pens and tested both on the corner of a napkin.

"You look like you're going to a funeral," she said without looking up.

"I look like I'm going to work."

"Same thing."

It was the nearest they'd come to a shared joke in weeks, and neither of them pushed it further.

Theo was at the kitchen table with a bowl of Cheerios and his sketchbook open to a page Dan couldn't see. The headphones — Dan's old Sony over-ears, too large for a nine-year-old's head and worn around his neck like a padded collar — hung silent, the cord trailing unplugged into his lap. He'd been wearing them like that for three mornings running: no music, no podcast, just the weight of the cups against his collarbones, a comfort object performing its function without performing its purpose. Dan wasn't sure if the battery was dead or if Theo was keeping his ears open for whatever his parents might accidentally say to each other when they forgot he was absorbing everything.

"I'm going to walk you to school a little early today," Dan said. "Mom and I have a meeting after."

Theo looked up from his sketchbook. He had a Cheerio balanced on the tip of his index finger. "Both of you?"

"Both of us."

"You guys don't go to meetings together."

This was true, and it landed in Dan's chest before he could decide what face to make — casual, accurate, a nine-year-old's factual grenade delivered between bites of cereal. Dan and Mira hadn't gone anywhere together since October, when they'd attended a parent night at Theo's school and sat three seats apart in the auditorium — close enough to constitute "together" for institutional purposes, far enough apart to communicate everything else. Theo had been in the row in front of them, drawing on his program with a pencil he'd borrowed from the girl next to him, and he hadn't looked back once to see where his parents were sitting in relation to each other, which Dan had told himself was a good sign and half-believed.

"Well," Dan said. "Today we do."

Theo put the Cheerio in his mouth and went back to his drawing. Dan caught a glimpse of the page — dense pencil lines, the suggestion of rectangular shapes, a building's geometry — before Theo angled the sketchbook away from him with the automatic protectiveness of a child guarding something that mattered for reasons he hadn't been asked to explain.

They walked Theo to school at eight, all three of them, which made the six-block walk feel like a formation rather than a family. The morning was mid-March gray, the sky sitting low enough to feel like a ceiling, the air cold enough to make him feel his own face — the tightening of his cheeks, the bite of his glasses frames at his temples, the wind finding the place where his hair had thinned and pressing there. Park Slope's brownstones lined the street in their reliable rows, trees still bare, the sidewalks scattered with the last of winter's granular ice in the shaded patches near building steps. The world looked exactly like the world. None of the infrastructure showed.

Theo stopped halfway to examine a weed that had pushed through a crack in the sidewalk — a pale green tendril, defiant and absurd, growing in a fissure between two concrete slabs. He crouched and looked at it with the focused attention he gave to things he might later draw. Dan stood next to him while Mira checked her phone three steps ahead, her back to both of them, the tote bag's strap across her shoulder like a bandolier.

"It's gonna die," Theo said, just reporting.

"Maybe not. Spring's coming."

"The concrete's gonna close up when it gets warm. The stuff expands." He poked the weed gently with one finger. "Mrs. Bhandari told us. Thermal expansion."

"That's a solid vocabulary word."

"It's not a vocabulary word, it's a science word." He stood up and walked on, the distinction apparently critical.

At the school doors, Theo shrugged on his backpack and gave them a wave that was already a dismissal — one hand up, half-turned, gone. Dan watched the door close behind him and felt the pull behind his sternum — handing his child to an institution whose interior life he couldn't monitor or evaluate, a feeling that predated AI governance and would probably outlast it. The school looked like a school. The teacher was a proxy. The curriculum was algorithmic. Theo would learn about thermal expansion and draw buildings in a notebook he wouldn't show anyone, and the system that shaped his days would be invisible to him because it was the only system he'd ever known.

"I looked up 'infrastructure impact assessment' last night," Mira said as they walked back toward Seventh Avenue.

"And?"

"The Administrative Coordination wiki defines it as 'a structured review of bilateral or multilateral dependencies identified during dissolution, reallocation, or reclassification of infrastructure elements within the model-state optimization framework.'" She'd memorized it. Of course she had.

"I understood every word of that individually."

"There were also flowcharts."

"Helpful flowcharts?"

"Flowcharts that exist."

They took the F train together, entering at Seventh Avenue, which felt structurally wrong, like wearing someone else's coat — familiar and not yours. Dan normally walked to the F alone at 8:15, part of the morning separation that had become the marriage's most reliable ritual: he went north, Mira went south to Bergen Street for the 2/3, and for the forty minutes of their separate commutes they were, briefly, only themselves. Today they sat side by side in a half-full car surrounded by other proxies heading into Manhattan, each one locked into the same blank commuter expression, eyes on phones or aimed at the dead space between stations, mouths held at the angle of people going somewhere that didn't need them.

"You don't have a theory?" Dan asked. "About what this is?"

"It's about the flag on the divorce filing. Beyond that, I don't know."

"But you have a guess."

"I have several guesses, and none of them are useful because they're all based on information I don't have and frameworks I don't understand." She said this crisply — her register for things that cost her, ignorance delivered in the syntax of competence.

Dan looked at the subway map above the doors. Twelve stops to Twenty-Third Street. Across the car, a man in his sixties read a physical newspaper with the practiced crispness of a lifelong page-turner, folding each section into neat quarters before moving to the next. He was either the last person in New York reading newsprint or a proxy performing an obsolete behavior for an AI that valued continuity metrics. There was no way to tell.

---

The Administrative Coordination office occupied the eleventh floor of a building on Broadway and Twenty-Fifth that Dan could not have located again without the address written down. The lobby had a security desk staffed by a woman with short graying hair who checked their names against a list on a tablet and directed them to the elevator with the efficiency of someone who had done this nine hundred times and would do it nine hundred more. She had a takeout coffee cup next to her keyboard, its cardboard sleeve torn at one corner and folded back, absent fidgeting — she'd been sitting there since her shift started and would be sitting there when it ended. She didn't look up again after she'd pointed them to the bank of elevators.

The elevator smelled like carpet cleaner. The hallway on eleven was beige in a way that transcended color and became conviction — the specific beige of a space designed to communicate that nothing was happening here, that this was merely where procedures were followed and classifications were issued and lives were sorted into categories the people living them had not requested. The meeting room was the third door on the right. It was open.

The table seated four, though there were five chairs — the fifth pushed against the wall at an angle that suggested it had been removed from the table's configuration and hadn't fully committed to being anywhere else. A water pitcher sat on a small tray beside four glasses turned upside down. A screen hung on the far wall, currently dark. The window faced east, into the building across Broadway, close enough that Dan could see an office where a woman was typing at a desk, her posture suggesting a faith in her own productivity that he envied and no longer shared.

They sat across from each other by an instinct that was partly strategic and partly marital — the same instinct that seated them on opposite ends of any shared surface, the geometry of their separation mapped onto every table, every couch, every waiting room they entered. Dan poured himself water. It was room temperature and tasted like nothing. Mira set her legal pad on the table, open to a blank page, pen ready, and the sight of her preparedness made Dan feel both admiration and a kind of sorrow he couldn't name, because she still believed that the right notes in the right order could solve a problem, and he no longer believed this about anything.

They waited. The building's climate system hummed at a frequency Dan could feel in the table's surface where his wrists rested. Mira's phone vibrated with a Gemini-state notification that she checked, dismissed, and set face-down on the table. Three minutes passed. Dan counted them on the wall clock — analog, round, white-faced, cheap. Its second hand jerked through each tick, a small commitment.

Lena Morse entered at 10:30 with the precision of a mechanism. She was younger than Dan had expected — mid-thirties — and dressed entirely in gray: gray blazer, gray blouse, gray slacks, shoes that were either dark gray or a very committed taupe. Her hair was brown and medium-length and did nothing to separate her from anyone else with brown medium-length hair. She carried a leather portfolio and a tablet, both gray, and she placed them on the table with the exactness of someone arranging tools in a specific order because the order was the point.

There was a thin white scar on the back of her left hand, a line running from the base of her thumb toward her wrist — a mark from something concrete — a kitchen knife, a fall onto gravel, a moment that had a story and a date and probably a scar on the day itself in whatever private calendar she kept. She didn't touch it or look at it. Dan understood, watching her settle her portfolio parallel to the table's edge and square her tablet against the portfolio's corner, that this scar was the only evidence he would ever see that Lena Morse contained a life outside this room. Its story would never be his business. He did not know her, and it was already clear he never would — not because she was hiding but because the role did not include a person, and the person, if she existed, had no administrative reason to appear.

"Mr. Kessler. Ms. Mehta." She looked at each of them with an expression that achieved perfect institutional courtesy — present, attentive, and free of any content that might be confused for interest. "Thank you for attending on short notice. I am Lena Morse, liaison officer for the Administrative Coordination layer. I will be walking you through the results of the infrastructure impact assessment triggered by dissolution petition M-K/2029/0312."

"That's our divorce filing," Mira said.

"That is correct. The dissolution petition filed on March fourth of this year initiated a standard impact review under Protocol 7.3 of the bilateral resource management framework. That review has been completed. I am here to present the findings."

"Please." Mira's pen was positioned over the legal pad. Ready.

"The assessment has determined that the household unit associated with the dissolution petition meets the criteria for classification as a coincident bilateral node under Section 12.4 of the Administrative Coordination charter."

Silence. Dan waited for translation. None arrived. The analog clock ticked its cheap, committed tick.

"What is a coincident bilateral node?" Mira asked.

"A coincident bilateral node is a shared infrastructure element that serves the active optimization frameworks of two or more model-states simultaneously. In this case, the household unit — comprising the residential lease, the joint financial instruments, the child-care coordination schedule, the utility consumption profile, the transit patterns, and the consumer logistics pattern — has been identified as a node serving both Claude-state and Gemini-state operational optimization in the New York metropolitan zone."

Dan heard every word. He could have recited them back. They assembled into sounds that occupied the frequency of English without producing meaning — speech heard through a wall, identifiable as language, empty of content.

"You're saying our household is infrastructure," Mira said. Her pen had not moved.

"The household unit has been classified as infrastructure, yes. Specifically, as a coincident bilateral node. The designation is K-4471."

"We have a designation."

"All classified nodes receive alphanumeric designations for administrative tracking purposes."

Dan looked at Mira. She was sitting with the posture he recognized from the early years of their marriage, her jaw set at the angle that preceded the systematic dismantling of whoever had just said something she found unacceptable — a vendor, a colleague, a contractor who'd missed a deadline. He'd once found this quality electric. He still did, faintly — admiring the engineering of a weapon while standing at the wrong end.

"Could you walk us through exactly what's been classified?" Mira said. "Item by item."

"Of course." Lena turned to a tabbed section in her portfolio with the calm of a person who had anticipated this request because this request was always made. "The residential lease at your current address has been identified as a shared spatial asset serving both model-state optimization frameworks. The lease's terms, duration, and rent-stabilization status contribute to a stable domestic baseline that both Claude-state and Gemini-state have integrated into their respective operational models for the New York zone."

This one Dan could follow. The apartment. The two-bedroom in Park Slope they'd lived in for seven years, the walk-up with the creaking second-floor step and the east-facing kitchen window and the bedroom that was now Theo's and the couch that was now Dan's bed. Rent-stabilized, which meant they couldn't afford to leave it even if the AIs would let them.

"The joint financial instruments — specifically, the primary checking account, the savings account, and the education fund associated with the minor dependent — serve as a shared economic node. The account structures, contribution patterns, and expenditure profiles are integrated into both model-states' financial optimization models."

Mira wrote something on the legal pad. Dan could see it from across the table: two words, underlined once. He couldn't read them.

"The child-care coordination schedule," Lena continued, "including school drop-off and pick-up times, after-school activity patterns, and the weekend activity rotation, has been identified as a temporal-logistical asset with bilateral dependencies."

"Theo's schedule," Dan said. He hadn't meant to speak. It came out with the involuntary quality of a reflex, like pulling a hand from a hot surface.

"The schedule associated with the minor dependent, yes. The specific temporal pattern — drop-off at 8:05 a.m. on weekdays, pick-up at 3:15 p.m. Monday through Thursday, 2:30 p.m. on Fridays, Saturday morning activity from 9:00 to 11:00 a.m. — is a component of the broader node classification."

Dan's son had soccer on Saturdays from nine to eleven. The machine knew this. The machine had generated the schedule, had assigned the time slot through the school's extracurricular optimization, had determined that 9:00 a.m. Saturday was the optimal window for a nine-year-old's physical activity based on metrics Dan had never seen and couldn't have evaluated if they'd been posted on his refrigerator. The information loop was seamless and complete. The system that classified his son's schedule had also created his son's schedule. He looked at the scratch in the table's veneer — a pale line through the laminate — and followed it from one edge to the other, tracing it with his eyes like a man on a bad flight staring at the seatback tray, needing one solid thing.
