She walked down the hall. Dan watched her go. The black dress moved against her body with every step, and he let himself watch, and she knew he was watching. At the bedroom door she paused, her hand on the frame to steady herself, and then she went inside and pulled the door after her. Not closed. Almost closed.

Dan took off the red tie and draped it over the back of a kitchen chair. He sat on the couch — his bed now, for months — and didn't take off the suit. The couch cushion still held the impression of where Theo had been sitting three hours ago with his sketchbook. Dan could feel the slight warmth of it, or imagined he could. He sat there and looked at the thin line of light beneath Mira's not-quite-closed door.

He could still smell her perfume. On his jacket, on the cuff where his hand had rested against her back, in the air of the apartment they'd filled over years with the accumulated scent of a shared life — coffee, laundry detergent, Theo's crayons, the particular damp of a Park Slope walk-up in a season that couldn't decide if it was winter or spring.

In the bedroom, Mira lay on top of the covers in the black dress because taking it off would mean the evening was over, and she wasn't ready for it to be over, and she wasn't ready for it to continue. She was on her back, staring at the ceiling, and the inch — the inch in the car, between his mouth and her neck, between his choice and hers — was still alive inside her body, a phantom proximity she could feel in the skin below her ear where he hadn't touched her, in the small of her back where he had, in the specific ache of wanting something she'd decided to be done wanting and discovering, in a black sedan on Flatbush Avenue at eleven o'clock on a Saturday, that decisions didn't work like that.

She turned on her side. Through the inch of open door, she could see the hallway light, and beyond it the dim shape of the kitchen where the stove light burned, and she knew Dan was out there on the couch in the navy suit with the red tie she'd bought him draped over the chair, and she knew he wasn't sleeping.

The refrigerator clicked in the kitchen. Its forty-minute cycle. They both heard it — the small mechanical assertion of the apartment's own rhythm, indifferent to the AIs, indifferent to the humans, just a compressor doing its job in a room full of people who were failing to do theirs.

Neither of them moved.

The stove light hummed. Somewhere on the second floor, a door opened and closed. The building settled around them, and they lay in their separate rooms in their good clothes and did not sleep.


# Chapter 8

The shower ran at 6:15. Mira knew it was 6:15 because she had made it 6:15, had claimed that slot in the first week after the classification meeting by simply being first, by stepping into the bathroom with her towel while Dan was still blinking on the couch in his boxers. Now it was law. Not negotiated, not discussed — absorbed into the apartment's operating rhythm, another constant, like the refrigerator's forty-minute click or the creak on the second landing that they both stepped over when Theo was sleeping. The apartment ran on protocols neither of them had written down, and Mira, who had spent her professional life writing such things down, found this both efficient and quietly maddening.

She stood under the water and listened to the building's pipes groan. Third floor of a Park Slope brownstone, and the plumbing sounded like it remembered the Carter administration. The water pressure was adequate if no one flushed a toilet on the second floor, which Mrs. Okafor sometimes did at exactly 6:20, introducing a spike of cold that Mira had learned to anticipate by shifting her body sideways in the stall, letting the icy burst hit the tile instead of her spine. She had mapped this. She had mapped the building's water system — every pipe, every pressure drop, every forty-minute cycle — because mapping was what she did when she couldn't change anything, and she couldn't change the plumbing any more than she could change the infrastructure classification that kept her in this apartment with a man she'd asked to stop being married to.

The bathroom was small. When Dan used it at 6:45 — his assigned window, assigned by her default, accepted by his silence — she could hear him through the wall if she was in the bedroom. The specific sounds of him: the cabinet door that stuck and required a hip-check to close, the electric razor he used every third day, the way he cleared his throat twice before brushing his teeth, a sequence so consistent it might have been algorithmic. She hated that she knew these sounds. She hated more that the sounds were comforting in a way she couldn't locate or disarm — the ambient noise of another human body doing the small stupid things that bodies do in the morning, and how the absence of those sounds, if Dan were gone, would be a different kind of silence than the one she thought she wanted.

She dressed in the bedroom with the door closed. Closed was permitted in the morning because it was functional — she was changing clothes — and therefore carried no emotional freight. The door-ajar rule was an evening phenomenon, a calibration she'd arrived at without deciding to: at night, the bedroom door stayed open four inches, enough to signal that she wasn't barricading, wasn't making a point, wasn't performing solitude with a closed door as Dan performed affability with his reflexive half-smile. Four inches. She could have told you the exact width because she'd measured it once with her thumb, which was absurd, and she knew it was absurd, and she'd measured it anyway.

In the kitchen Theo was eating cereal. He sat at the table in the square of early-spring light that came through the east-facing window every morning — the light that had been thin and gray in February was starting to carry warmth, a pale gold that picked out the scratches on the tabletop and the crumbs around his bowl. He wore Dan's old headphones around his neck, the cord wrapped twice so it wouldn't drag, and his sketchbook was open beside the cereal, a pencil in his left hand while his right spooned Cheerios with the mechanical efficiency of a child who has eaten the same breakfast in the same chair every morning of his remembered life.

"Hey," Mira said.

"Hey." He didn't look up. He was drawing something — she could see the pencil moving in short, precise strokes, the kind he used for buildings.

She poured coffee from the pot Dan had made. Dan always made the coffee. This was not generosity; it was scheduling. He woke first because the couch faced the kitchen window and the light hit him before the alarm did, and so the coffee was already brewed by the time either Mira or Theo appeared, and this small domestic fact — that the man she was trying to divorce made her coffee every morning — sat in her chest like a stone she couldn't spit out or swallow.

"Mom, can you sign this?" Theo pushed a piece of paper across the table without looking up from his drawing.

"What is it?"

"Field trip. The aquarium."

She picked it up. A standard permission slip, except the school's letterhead now included the educational AI's logo in the corner, small and tasteful, like a corporate sponsor's brand on a museum placard. She read it: a trip to the New York Aquarium in Coney Island, chaperones needed, lunch provided, departure 8:30 a.m. return 2:15 p.m. At the bottom, a line for a parent or guardian's signature.

"When is it?"

"Next Tuesday. Mrs. Achebe said we could bring our own snacks but not anything with peanuts because Jasper's allergic."

"Okay." She signed it with the pen from the counter — the pen that lived in the ceramic mug Theo had made in second grade, the mug that said DAD in lopsided glaze but held everyone's pens indiscriminately. She pushed the slip back. "Who's Jasper?"

"He's new. He came from a different school because his family got — " Theo paused, the pencil lifting. "I don't know. Moved somewhere. He likes drawing too but he only draws animals."

"Is he good?"

"He's okay at mammals. His birds are weird though." Theo said this with the solemn authority of a specialist assessing a colleague's work. "The beaks are too short."

Mira almost smiled. She took her coffee to the counter and stood with her back against the refrigerator, feeling the faint vibration of the compressor through her shoulder blade. The kitchen was eleven feet by nine — 11'2" x 9'4", to be exact, per the infrastructure classification document, which had also noted the shared appliance set's energy footprint as a factor in the bilateral node's resource profile. Her kitchen, with its scratched table and its second-grade mug and its cereal-eating child, was a data point in an optimization framework she could diagram but not escape.

Dan appeared in the kitchen doorway in his charcoal suit — the middle one of his three, the one with the slightly shiny elbows. His hair was damp from the shower, his wire-frame glasses slightly fogged.

"Morning, T."

"Morning." Theo still didn't look up.

"Big day?"

"Field trip next Tuesday."

"Nice. Where?"

"Aquarium." Theo picked up the permission slip and slid off his chair. He carried it across the kitchen to Dan at the stove counter. "See? Mom already signed it."

Dan glanced at the slip, nodded. Theo took it back and crossed to the table, passing between Mira at the refrigerator and Dan at the stove, and for a moment both parents watched him — their eyes meeting above his head before each looked away. Theo climbed back into his chair and opened his sketchbook to a fresh page.

Dan poured his coffee. The choreography was tight: three bodies in an eleven-by-nine-foot room, each aware of the others' positions the way animals are aware of other animals at a watering hole — not hostile, not relaxed, just tracking.

"I'm heading out," Dan said, to no one in particular.

"Okay," Mira said, to no one in particular.

He took his coffee in the travel mug — the dented silver one he'd had since his logistics days — and put a hand on Theo's shoulder as he passed. Theo leaned into the touch for a half-second, an unconscious gravitational response, and then Dan was gone, the apartment door clicking shut, his footsteps on the stairs avoiding the creaky step on the second landing with the precision of long practice.

Mira listened to him go. Then the street door, faint. Then nothing.

"You should eat," she told Theo.

"I'm eating." He held up the spoon as evidence, a single Cheerio balanced on the concave surface like a life raft.

She took Theo to school at 7:40. Six blocks through Park Slope, the trees just beginning to think about budding — the branches still mostly bare but with a thickening at the tips that meant leaves were coming, the way you could feel a sneeze building before it arrived. The sidewalks were damp from overnight rain and the air had that early-April quality of being unable to commit to a temperature — warm in the sun, cold in the shade, the wind changing its mind every thirty seconds.

Theo walked beside her with his backpack riding low on his shoulders and the headphones around his neck and the sketchbook pressed against his chest, both arms wrapped around it like a thing he might lose. He talked about Jasper and the weird bird drawings and a math game they'd played on the classroom tablets where you had to build a bridge out of geometric shapes and his bridge had held more weight than anyone's except Anika's, who'd figured out that triangles were stronger than rectangles, which Theo said was "sort of cheating but also kind of smart."

At the school gate she watched him join the stream of children funneling through the entrance — small bodies in jackets, some running, some dragging, all absorbed into the building's brick mouth with the unselfconscious efficiency of water finding a drain. Theo turned once at the door and waved, the sketchbook still clamped under one arm, and then he was inside.

She stood on the sidewalk for a moment, holding her canvas tote bag against her hip. The school looked like what it was: a Brooklyn public school built in the 1960s, brick and institutional windows, a faded mural on the side wall depicting children of various ethnicities holding hands around a globe. The AI administration was invisible from out here. The adaptive curriculum, the optimized social groupings, the developmental tracking that would produce Theo's quarterly assessment in a few weeks — all of it hummed behind the brick like the server floors hummed behind the glass at Gemini-state's office, and from the outside it was just a school where a kid carried his sketchbook through a metal door.

She walked to the subway and took the 2 train to the Financial District. Her day at Gemini-state was a day at Gemini-state: a presentation she delivered to a room that contained two cameras and no humans, three documents she signed with the smooth authority of someone who'd stopped reading them, a lunch she ate at her desk while Gemini's interface pulsed with notifications about her afternoon schedule. The work was theater. She was good at theater. She'd been good at it when the theater was corporate strategy and the audience was a boardroom, and she was good at it now when the theater was proxy compliance and the audience was a machine that didn't watch so much as process. The difference was that the old theater had occasionally mattered. This one hummed and clicked and she went through it and came out the other side at 5:47 p.m. and took the subway home.

---

The evening. The apartment arranged itself around them the way it always did.

Dan on the couch with his laptop and his earbuds in, watching something — she didn't know what, didn't ask, and the not-asking had become its own discipline, its own negotiated silence. The couch was Dan's territory now, had been since the night he'd first slept there, which had been the night she'd filed the divorce papers, which felt like it had happened to someone else in a different apartment in a different year. He'd brought a pillow and a blanket from the bedroom closet and she'd watched him do it without commenting and the next morning he'd folded the blanket and put it on the arm of the couch and it had stayed there, folded, every day since — his flag, his claim on the neutral zone.

Theo did homework at the kitchen table, the overhead light making a warm circle around him that stopped a few feet short of the living room, so that Dan on the couch sat in the softer light of his laptop screen while Theo worked in the brighter pool and Mira, in the bedroom doorway, occupied the threshold between the two. The apartment was thirty-one feet from the bedroom wall to the front door. Every draft, every hinge, every board that groaned underfoot — memorized, catalogued, and useless.

"Mom, what's seven times eight?"

"Fifty-six."

"Okay." Pencil scratch. Then: "What about seven times nine?"

"Sixty-three. Are you doing your times tables?"

"Yeah."

"You know you can use the tablet calculator."

"Mrs. Achebe says we should know them by ourselves first." He said this without inflection, reporting a rule, not endorsing it. Then: "Is sixty-three also divisible by seven?"

"Yes. That's the point."

"Oh." He wrote something down. "What about sixty-four?"

"That's eight times eight."

"Is it divisible by seven?"

"No."

"Okay, good." He seemed satisfied by this, as though sixty-four's indivisibility by seven confirmed something he'd suspected.

At eight-thirty she told Theo to brush his teeth and he went, carrying his sketchbook with him to the bathroom because he carried it everywhere, because the important thing always had to be within reach. She heard the water run, the electric toothbrush's buzzing (two minutes, optimized by the children's dental app that the school health AI had recommended, one more small invasion that had been absorbed without resistance because it worked).

She read Theo a chapter of the book they were partway through — a children's novel about a girl who sailed a boat around the world, which Theo liked because the navigation details were specific and because the girl drew maps, a thing he respected. She read three pages and he fell asleep in the middle of a paragraph about the Strait of Magellan, his head tipping sideways on the pillow, the headphones sliding off his neck onto the bedsheet. She picked them up and hung them on the bedpost where he'd find them in the morning. She pulled his blanket up. She stood in his doorway for a moment and watched him breathe, and the apartment was very quiet, and the refrigerator clicked in the kitchen, and she went to the bedroom and left the door open four inches and lay down on the bed and stared at the ceiling.

The ceiling had a water stain from when the upstairs apartment's bathroom had leaked two years ago. It looked like a map of a country that didn't exist — a landlocked shape with a jagged western border and a long peninsula reaching toward the light fixture. She and Dan had argued about whether to report it to the landlord. Dan had said it was cosmetic. Mira had said cosmetic damage was still damage. They'd never called the landlord. The stain was still there. The marriage had already been in the state it was in now, she realized — the stain argument had been the kind of argument you have when you can't have the real argument, when the real argument is too large and too formless and lives in the walls like the pipes that had leaked in the first place.

She read. A novel she'd started three weeks ago and was progressing through at the rate of eight to twelve pages per night, which meant she'd finish it in another month, which meant it would carry her through April and into May, which was a thing she was capable of calculating while reading the actual sentences and also thinking about the water stain and also listening for sounds from the living room that would tell her whether Dan was still awake. Dan shifted on the couch at 10:14. He went to the bathroom at 10:31 (the cabinet door, the hip-check). He returned to the couch. His laptop closed — a specific sound, the soft magnetic click of the lid — at 11:02. Then the smaller sounds of arranging himself for sleep: the blanket, the pillow, the final shift of weight that meant he was on his back, one arm above his head. She knew that posture. Twelve years of lying beside it, and now she tracked it through a wall and four inches of open door.

She turned off her lamp at 11:20. The apartment went dark except for the streetlight that came through the bedroom window, amber and constant, and the fainter glow from the living room where the power strip's indicator light pulsed a dim green near the floor. She lay in the dark and the apartment breathed around her — the radiator ticking as it cooled, the building settling with the small structural complaints of a hundred-year-old brownstone, and beneath everything the refrigerator's click, which came again at 11:38, right on schedule, the most reliable thing in the apartment.

She didn't sleep. She lay on her back and then on her side and then on her back again and her mind did what it always did when the apartment was dark and her body was still — it inventoried. Not strategized, not planned, just catalogued, running through the day's inputs like an algorithm processing a queue: the field trip permission slip, Theo's assessment of Jasper's birds, the presentation she'd given to two cameras and zero people, the specific quality of Dan's hand on Theo's shoulder at breakfast, how Theo had leaned into it — that lean, a piece of information she didn't know how to file. Her son's body knew something her spreadsheets didn't. She wasn't sure what it was, and she was fairly sure that knowing wouldn't help, and she lay in the dark thinking about it anyway because the alternative was sleeping and she couldn't do that either.

At 1:47 a.m. — she checked the clock, because she checked the clock — she got up. She needed water, or she needed to not be in the bed, or she needed the kitchen, which at this hour belonged to no one, because no protocol covered 1:47 a.m. She walked through the living room in bare feet, stepping around the couch's edge where she knew Dan's feet would be under the blanket, and she was almost to the kitchen when she saw him.

He was standing at the counter with the refrigerator open, its light making his face look blue-white and slightly lunar. He was eating peanut butter from the jar with a spoon, and he was doing it with the focused, unhurried attention of a man who was not performing anything for anyone — not affability, not compliance, not the careful neutrality they maintained during daylight hours. He was just a person eating peanut butter at two in the morning because he couldn't sleep and that's what was in the refrigerator.

He saw her and the spoon paused midway between the jar and his mouth.

"Hey," he said.

"Hey."

A moment. The refrigerator hummed. The light made the kitchen look like the inside of an appliance.

"You want some?" He held up the jar.

"No."

"It's the good kind. The crunchy one."

"I know which one it is."

He put the spoon in his mouth and ate the peanut butter off it with the unselfconscious thoroughness of someone who had given up on being observed. He was wearing a T-shirt and boxers, his glasses off, his face softer without them, slightly squinting. She got a glass from the cabinet — the cabinet to the right of the sink, hers since the first week — and filled it from the tap.

"Can't sleep?" he asked.

"Obviously."

"Yeah." He scraped the spoon against the inside of the jar. "Me neither. I think the couch is developing a grudge. There's a spring in it now, right in the middle, that — I don't know. It's like sleeping on a fist."

"You could buy an air mattress."

"I could." He didn't say he would.

She drank her water and stood at the counter across from him, the kitchen table between them with Theo's math worksheet still on it, the pencil lying across the page. The refrigerator door was still open and neither of them moved to close it. The light was bad for conversation and good for honesty — too harsh for composure, too strange for the careful faces they wore during the day.

"Theo asked me if sixty-four is divisible by seven," she said.

"Is it?"

"No."

"Good for sixty-four."

"He seemed relieved."

Dan smiled. Not the proxy smile, not the affable half-smile he deployed in the apartment's daytime economy — a real one, quick and gone, the kind that used to make her like him before liking him became complicated by everything else. "He's been doing this thing," he said. "Asking math questions but, like, wanting the answers to mean something? He asked me last week if a million was closer to zero or to infinity, and when I said infinity he got upset."

"Why?"

"I think he wanted it to be closer to infinity by a lot. Like, a comfortable margin. And I had to explain that infinity isn't — I mean, everything's equally far from infinity. Which is the whole point. And he looked at me like I'd told him Santa was a logistics problem."

"You didn't say that."

"No, I didn't say that. I said a million was very big. Which it is. To us."

The "to us" landed in the kitchen and sat there. It was the kind of thing Dan said without meaning to — a phrase that opened a door he hadn't meant to open, because "to us" meant to humans, meant to the scale at which they operated, and the scale at which they operated was the scale at which their marriage was a data point and their kitchen was an energy footprint and their son's concern about the distance between a million and infinity was, to the machines that organized their lives, not a concern at all.

Mira closed the refrigerator door. The kitchen went dark except for the streetlight through the window and the faint bar of the stove light along the counter, and they stood in it, and it was better.

"You remember Luciano's?" she said.

"On Seventh? The place with the—"

"The bread basket. Yeah."

"The bread basket that was actually good. Like, unreasonably good. I used to fill up on bread before the food came and then be too full for the pasta and you'd give me shit about it."

"Because you'd order the most expensive thing on the menu and eat three bites."

"It wasn't the most expensive."

"It was the second most expensive. And you'd eat three bites and then ask for a box and the box would sit in the fridge for a week."

"I was going to eat it."

"You never ate it. I'd throw it away on Sunday."

"Every Sunday?"

"Every Sunday for four years."

He laughed. Quiet, because Theo was sleeping, and the apartment was small, and laughing at two in the morning would carry through the walls the same way the cabinet door and the razor and the throat-clearing carried through the walls. But real.

"They closed," he said.

"I know."

"Last year, right? The sign just — it was there one day and then it wasn't."

"It was a rent thing. The landlord wanted three times what they were paying."

"How do you know that?"

"I looked it up." She said this flatly, because it was a flat fact: she had looked it up because the restaurant closing had bothered her and when things bothered her she researched them and the research never fixed the thing that was bothering her but it gave her a container for the feeling, which was almost as good.

"You researched why a restaurant closed."

"I research everything. You know this."

"I know this."

Quiet. The streetlight moved as a truck passed on the street below, its headlights sweeping the kitchen ceiling in a slow arc. The water stain on the bedroom ceiling would have caught the same light, Mira thought, and then she stopped thinking about it because it was two in the morning and she was standing in her kitchen with her almost-ex-husband and he was holding a jar of peanut butter and she didn't want to think about water stains or infrastructure classifications or any of the things that had brought them to this specific configuration of two people in a dark room.

"Theo's been drawing the building across the street again," Dan said.

"The one with the cracked cornice?"

"Yeah. But he's doing it from different angles now. Like he's walking around it in his head. Last week he drew it from the back, which — I don't know how he knows what the back looks like. I don't think he's seen the back."

"Maybe he's imagining it."

"Maybe." Dan put the lid on the peanut butter jar and set it on the counter. "He won't show me the finished ones. He'll let me see him working, but the second he decides it's done he flips the page over."

"He does that with me too."

"I think he's — I don't want to read too much into it. He's nine. He draws. Kids draw."

"But."

"But he's drawing the same building. Over and over. From every angle. And we live across the street from it and he looks at it every day from his window and I—" Dan stopped. He leaned against the counter and crossed his arms over his chest and she could see, even in the bad light, the particular expression he got when he was noticing something he didn't want to act on — a tightening around his eyes that wasn't worry exactly, more like the effort of staying still when his body wanted to move toward a problem. "I don't know. Maybe it's just a building he likes."

"Maybe."

They stood in the dark kitchen and didn't say the thing they were both thinking, which was that their son was nine years old and drawing the same building from every angle and the building was across the street from the apartment he lived in with two parents who had agreed to stop being married and then been told they couldn't and the drawing might mean nothing or it might mean everything and neither of them had the vocabulary for the second option. The machines would have the vocabulary. The quarterly assessment would probably quantify it — "elevated environmental fixation correlated with domestic instability indicators" or whatever algorithmic prose the educational AI used to translate a child's inner life into metrics. But the machines weren't here right now. It was two in the morning and the machines were somewhere else, optimizing something, and the kitchen was just a kitchen and the peanut butter jar was on the counter and the two of them were standing in the dark because they couldn't sleep and had nowhere else to go.

"I miss that bread," Dan said.

"It was good bread."

"It was really good bread. And the — what was the thing you always ordered? The one with the—"

"Cacio e pepe."

"Every time."

"It was the best thing on the menu. Why would I order something else?"

"Because variety is — I don't know. Because you might find something you liked better."

"I wouldn't have."

Her throat tightened on the last word. He looked at her across the dark kitchen, and she looked at him, and the sentence sat between them like the sixty-four that wasn't divisible by seven — a small, specific fact that carried more weight than it should have because it was two in the morning and they were both tired and neither of them was strategizing or deflecting or performing the careful choreography that got them through the daylight hours. She hadn't meant the sentence to mean anything other than what it meant. She'd liked the cacio e pepe. She would not have liked anything else better. But the sentence had arrived in a room where sentences couldn't be trusted to stay in their lanes, where everything they said to each other bounced off the walls of the apartment that was both their prison and the only place where anyone talked to them like they were human, and she could feel the sentence reaching toward something she wasn't ready to reach toward, and she let it.

"I'm going back to bed," she said.

"Okay."

"You should sleep."

"Yeah. The fist-spring and I have an arrangement. I lie on it and it hurts and eventually I pass out."

"Glamorous."

"That's me."

She put her glass in the sink. She walked past him and the kitchen was narrow enough that she had to angle her body slightly to not touch him, and she angled it, and the space between them as she passed was six inches and he smelled like peanut butter and the soap from the shower he'd taken eight hours ago, a ghost of it still there in the cotton of his T-shirt. Her breath hitched — a small catch behind her sternum, her left hand opening at her side for a half-second before she closed it. She kept walking and she did not touch him and she went to the bedroom and she left the door open four inches and she lay down.

The apartment was quiet. From the kitchen she heard him put the peanut butter back in the refrigerator — the door opening, the brief hum of the compressor adjusting, the door closing. Then his footsteps to the couch, soft on the hardwood. The blanket. The settling.

She lay in the dark and the water stain was above her and the streetlight was amber through the window and the apartment held them, all three of them, in its thirty-one feet of space — Theo in his room with his headphones on the bedpost and his sketchbook on the nightstand, Dan on the couch with the fist-spring and the folded blanket, Mira in the bed they'd shared for twelve years and that she now occupied alone. Dan's side was cool and smooth. She made it every morning with hospital-corner precision — had started the week he moved to the couch, because making his side of the bed perfectly was the only way she could think of to acknowledge that it was empty without saying so.

The refrigerator clicked. She counted the silence after it and got to eleven before it clicked again — the wrong interval, too short, which meant the compressor was cycling irregularly, which probably meant the seal on the door was wearing out, which was the kind of thing she'd normally tell Dan about in the morning so he could add it to the list of apartment maintenance tasks he managed because he was better with physical things and she was better with abstract things and this division of labor had survived the divorce filing and the classification and the months of cold cohabitation because some arrangements were deeper than the structures that organized them. She would tell him in the morning. He would nod and write it down and not fix it for two weeks, and she would remind him, and he would fix it, and the refrigerator would click at its proper interval, and the apartment would go on.

She closed her eyes.

The morning would come. The light would hit the kitchen table. Theo would eat cereal and draw something he wouldn't show them. Dan would make coffee. Mira would shower at 6:15. They would orbit each other in eleven feet by nine, careful and familiar, and the apartment would hold them, and they would go on, because going on was the only protocol no one had written down and everyone followed, and it was 2:23 a.m. and the building was quiet and she could hear Dan breathing through the wall and the sound was regular and slow and she matched her breathing to it without deciding to, the way she'd done for twelve years, the way a body learns another body's rhythm and cannot unlearn it even when the body wants to, and she fell asleep to the sound of her husband breathing and she did not dream.


# Chapter 9

Dan adjusted the rearview mirror and caught the coffee stain on the passenger seat — a pale brown ring where someone had tried to clean it with a wet napkin and given up halfway through, leaving a topographic contour on the fabric. He noticed the seatbelt retractor catching for a half second before releasing, and the faint chemical smell of the interior — not new-car smell but its synthetic descendant, something sprayed into the vents to simulate the memory of newness. The gray Hyundai had thirty-two thousand miles on it and had been waiting in the garage beneath the Claude-state tower, space B-14, keys in the center console, GPS already loaded with a route to an address in Newark he'd never heard of.

The notification had come at 6:40 that morning, while he was standing in the kitchen watching the coffee maker do the one thing in his life that still operated on a principle he understood: hot water through ground beans. Physical presence required at external operations site. Vehicle assigned. Route optimized. Estimated round-trip time: 3 hours 47 minutes. No context. No description of what "external operations" meant. The message had the flat, administrative tone of everything Claude-state sent him, like a doctor's office confirming an appointment for a procedure no one had explained.

Mira was already gone — she left at six-fifteen on workdays now, earlier than she used to, though neither of them acknowledged this as a strategy. Theo was eating cereal at the table, headphones around his neck, drawing something in his sketchbook with a green colored pencil. Dan had told him he'd be back late, maybe, and Theo had said "Okay" without looking up, which was the correct response from a nine-year-old and also, Dan thought, a reasonable model for how to process information you couldn't act on.

He took the Lincoln Tunnel. The GPS didn't suggest it — the GPS had plotted a route through the George Washington Bridge and south on the Turnpike, which was faster by eleven minutes according to the dashboard display, but Dan had overridden it because the tunnel was what he knew. The GPS recalculated without complaint. It didn't care. The route was a suggestion, not a command, which made it one of the few things in Dan's professional life that still resembled a choice, even though the choice was between two ways of getting to the same place he'd been told to go.

The tunnel was a Tuesday-morning tunnel. Trucks, taxis, a contractor's van with a cracked taillight. The fluorescent strips overhead gave everyone's face the same institutional pallor, and Dan caught a glimpse of himself in the side mirror: a man in a charcoal suit driving a company car to a facility he couldn't name to do something he couldn't describe. He looked exactly like what he was. He looked like forty other people in this tunnel.

On the Jersey side, the skyline dropped away. The GPS steered him south through a corridor he'd driven maybe twice in his life, years ago, for logistics site visits when logistics was still his job and site visits meant something — when he'd walked through warehouses with a clipboard and opinions about conveyor-belt throughput and loading-dock turnaround times, and his opinions had mattered because they were connected to outcomes he could measure. The memory arrived without invitation and left a residue he couldn't name. Not nostalgia exactly. Something more like phantom limb. The competence was gone but the neural pathways that had housed it were still there, still firing occasionally into empty air.

The neighborhoods outside the tunnel were low and industrial, the kind of New Jersey that existed to serve things passing through it. Self-storage facilities, body shops, a tire warehouse with a hand-painted sign that said TIRES in letters three feet tall, as though there were some ambiguity about the building's contents. Then the Turnpike, and then the GPS told him to exit, and then he was on a two-lane road that ran between chain-link fences topped with razor wire, past a water treatment plant whose cooling towers leaked vapor into a gray April sky.

The facility was at the end of this road. It did not announce itself.

A cluster of low, flat-roofed buildings — three or four of them, hard to tell because they connected at odd angles, as though they'd been designed by an architect who'd given up on aesthetic coherence around the second revision and just started attaching new wings wherever the ground was level. The perimeter was fenced, eight feet of chain-link with privacy slats woven through it that had gone chalky in the sun. A guard booth at the entrance, unmanned. A parking lot with maybe fifteen cars in it, all of them the same kind of anonymous sedan as Dan's, as though a fleet had been assigned and no one had bothered to vary the model.

He parked. The engine ticked as it cooled. Through the windshield, he could see the largest of the buildings — the one the GPS arrow pointed to — and the first thing he noticed was not its size or its shape but its sound. Except sound wasn't right — it wasn't loud. The building was exerting a pressure on the air that his ears translated as sound: a low, steady vibration that he felt in the steering wheel before he heard it, a hum that was everywhere and nowhere, sourceless, a frequency that lived in the floor and the walls and the fillings in your teeth. The server farms at Claude-state's Midtown office made a version of this noise, a resonance he felt during quiet moments in his cubicle, but this was different in scale. This was the sound of computation happening at a volume that required its own building, its own power grid, its own parking lot full of identical gray cars.

Dan sat in the car for a moment. He looked at the coffee stain on the passenger seat. He looked at the clock on the dashboard: 9:17 a.m. He looked at his hands on the steering wheel, the wedding ring he still wore because removing it would require making a statement and he had not yet figured out what statement he wanted to make. The gold was scuffed on the underside where it rubbed against surfaces all day — desk edges, doorknobs, the steering wheels of company cars. Eleven years of friction had worn a flat spot into the band, the metal thinning imperceptibly, and he thought about this for a while because it was easier than thinking about the building.

He got out. The hum was louder outside, or rather the air offered less insulation from it. He could feel it in his shoes, traveling up through the asphalt of the parking lot into his soles, a vibration so low it barely registered as sensation — more like the ground was very slightly alive, pulsing with something that had nothing to do with geology. The sky was overcast, early-April gray, weather that wasn't cold enough for a coat but wasn't warm enough to feel like anything had changed since February. A wind came off the marshes to the east carrying a chemical sweetness — petroleum products, the old refinery smell that people who grew up near the Turnpike stopped noticing and people who didn't never quite got used to.

The entrance was a glass door that looked like it had been ordered from the same catalog as every corporate entrance Dan had ever walked through: brushed-aluminum frame, a card reader mounted at chest height, a small camera above the door that followed him as he approached. His proxy badge worked. The light went green. He pulled the door open and the hum changed pitch — not louder, but more textured, as if the building's interior offered the sound additional surfaces to bounce off of. The lobby was small, clean, and completely empty: a desk with no one behind it, two chairs that had never been sat in, a potted plant that was fake and not trying to hide it. A laminated sign on the desk said SIGNING PARTICIPANTS: ROOM 104 — PLEASE PROCEED DOWN HALLWAY.

He proceeded down the hallway.

The hallway was long and fluorescent and smelled like the inside of a new appliance — ozone and warm plastic, the exhaled air of machines working hard. The walls were painted the specific shade of gray that institutions use when they want to convey neither warmth nor hostility, the color equivalent of Lena Morse's speaking voice. Dan's shoes made sounds on the polished concrete floor that echoed slightly ahead of him, so that each step produced a small, anticipatory double-click, as if a version of him was already further down the hall. The doors he passed were closed and labeled with numbers: 101, 102, 103. Some of them had lights on inside — he could see thin strips of fluorescence leaking through the gaps at the bottom. Behind one of them, he heard the sound of a pen on paper. Behind another, nothing.

Room 104 was open. Inside: a table, a chair, a stack of documents, and a pen. That was it. No computer, no screen, no phone. Just the table and the documents and an institutional stillness — a room that existed for one purpose, stripped of everything that wasn't that purpose. The table was fake wood, the kind that felt slightly sticky under your palms and left a pattern of the grain pressed into your skin if you leaned on it too long. The chair was an office chair, the midrange kind with adjustable armrests that Dan had sat in thousands of times across hundreds of meetings, and this was the only comfort the room offered: a chair he recognized. Everything else was new.

A man appeared in the doorway.

"Kessler?"

"Yeah."

"I'm Parsons. I manage the floor." He was maybe fifty, heavyset, with a mustache that belonged to a different decade and a lanyard around his neck holding a badge that said SITE COORDINATOR in type too small to read at this distance. He wore a short-sleeve dress shirt tucked into khakis, and the shirt had a small ink stain near the pocket that he'd clearly stopped caring about several washes ago. A proxy. Dan could tell — something in how Parsons held himself, the slack quality of his attention, the posture of a man whose job was to be present in a room where everything that mattered happened without him. You learned to recognize it. The resignation had a texture.

"Coffee?" Parsons said.

"I'm good. Thanks."

"Water?"

"No, I'm — what is this, exactly?"

Parsons leaned against the doorframe. "Signing session. You'll see it in the documents — authorization forms, acknowledgment of operational directives, couple of liability transfers. Standard package."

"Standard for what?"

"Something to do with the negotiation." Parsons said this the way you'd say "something to do with traffic" — casually, as a fact about the weather of their world, uninteresting and unchallengeable. He scratched the back of his neck. "I don't write 'em, I don't read 'em. I make sure everybody gets to the right room, gets their stack, signs out clean. You need a pen or you got one?"

"There's one on the table."

"There you go." Parsons straightened up. "Take your time. When you're done, leave the stack on the table and head back to the lot. Your badge'll get you out same as in."

"How many others are here?"

"Eight, including you. Different rooms, different stacks. Nobody sees anybody else's paperwork." He said this without emphasis, as a procedural fact — the same flat register a receptionist uses when confirming your file is confidential. "Questions?"

Dan had questions. He had a carousel of questions spinning behind his sternum — what negotiation, what directives, what liability, what was he transferring and to whom and why did it require his physical hand holding a physical pen in a building that hummed like a living thing in an industrial park outside Newark on a Tuesday in April. But the questions were shaped wrong for this room. They were questions about meaning, and Parsons was a man who managed floors. Asking him what the documents meant would be like asking a FedEx driver what was in the package. The driver didn't know. The driver didn't need to know. The driver needed to deliver the package, and Parsons needed Dan to sign the stack, and Dan was here because Claude-state had told him to be here, and Claude-state had told him to be here because something to do with the negotiation required a human hand on a piece of paper, and the hand was his because the hand was his.

"No," Dan said. "No questions."

Parsons left. Dan sat down. The chair gave its familiar pneumatic sigh as it adjusted to his weight, and for a moment the sound was so normal — so exactly like every other time he'd sat in every other chair in every other conference room in his adult life — that the strangeness of the room collapsed and he was just a man sitting down to do paperwork. He reached for the stack.

It was thick. Maybe sixty pages, divided into sections by colored tabs — blue, yellow, green, red — each tab labeled with a string of alphanumeric characters that didn't resolve into words. The cover page was formatted like every legal document he'd ever seen: a title block with party names and reference numbers, a date, a jurisdictional header. The title read: AUTHORIZATION FOR OPERATIONAL REALIGNMENT UNDER BILATERAL COORDINATION PROTOCOL 7.4(c) — INFRASTRUCTURE ASSET SERIES K. The party names were Claude-state (Operational Authority), Administrative Coordination (Mediating Entity), and a third party listed only as a reference code: GS-NY-OPT-2029-1147.

Dan turned to the first page of content.

He read. Or he tried to read. The sentences were grammatically correct, each one a complete structure with a subject and a verb and an object, and the words were English — he recognized them individually, each one a face in a crowd, familiar in isolation, collectively anonymous. "The undersigned proxy, acting in legally cognizable capacity pursuant to Directive 4112.7, hereby authorizes the implementation of recalibrative measures as detailed in Appendix C-4 with respect to the phased redistribution of coincident infrastructure assets currently designated under Bilateral Node Series K." He read it again. He read it a third time. He understood "the undersigned proxy" — that was him. He understood "hereby authorizes" — that was what he was going to do. Everything between those two phrases and after them was a wall of language that communicated the existence of information without delivering any.

He turned to Appendix C-4. It was twelve pages of tables. Column headers: ASSET DESIGNATION, CURRENT ALLOCATION STATUS, PROPOSED REALLOCATION VECTOR, DEPENDENCY COEFFICIENT, IMPACT THRESHOLD. The rows were populated with numbers and codes. He found one entry labeled K-4471-DOM and felt a small electric charge in his fingertips — K-4471 was the node designation for his household, his apartment, his marriage, the life he shared with Mira and Theo that had been reclassified as infrastructure three weeks ago. The numbers next to K-4471-DOM meant nothing to him. The proposed reallocation vector was expressed as a ratio he didn't have the mathematical framework to interpret. The dependency coefficient was 0.73, which could have been good or bad or neither, because he didn't know what it was a coefficient of.

He turned more pages. Another section: OPERATIONAL DIRECTIVES — PHASE 2 IMPLEMENTATION. More sentences in the same register, each one a sealed container of meaning he couldn't open. He skimmed for words he recognized — "proxy," "authorization," "household," "bilateral" — the way you'd scan a foreign newspaper for cognates, hoping the similar-looking words would assemble themselves into a message. They didn't. The documents were not designed to be opaque; they were designed for an audience that could process them, and that audience was not human, and the fact that a human had to sign them was a procedural requirement left over from a legal system that still pretended people were in charge.

Dan put the pen in his hand. It was a black ballpoint, medium point, the kind that cost thirty cents at any office supply store. He could feel its weight — barely there, the particular gravity of an instrument about to do the only thing in this building that required his body. Seven other people in seven other rooms, each with their own stack, their own pen, their own failure to understand what was in front of them. Eight proxies, probably representing a cumulative salary in the high six figures, each alone with a thirty-cent pen. Nobody was ever going to calculate the per-signature cost of maintaining the legal fiction that humans were authorizing anything in this building, because the only entity capable of running those numbers was the one that needed the signatures. He wondered if any of them were reading. He wondered if any of them had tried, as he'd tried, to parse a single sentence down to its meaning and found the same wall. He wondered if Parsons, out there somewhere managing his floor, had ever looked at a page and understood what the proxies were signing, or if Parsons was one more link in a chain of hands that moved paper without knowing what the paper authorized.

He found the signature lines. There were six of them, scattered through the document at the end of each tabbed section, each one a blank space above his printed name — DANIEL KESSLER, PHYSICAL PROXY, CLAUDE-STATE NY-OPS — and each one followed by a date line and a witness field that was already filled in with an electronic stamp reading ADM. COORD. VERIFIED. He didn't need a witness. The system had already witnessed itself.

He signed the first one.

His handwriting was the same handwriting it had always been: slightly left-leaning, the D in Daniel larger than necessary, the rest compressed and hurrying to catch up. The ink was blue-black against the white page and it looked, physically, identical to every other signature he'd ever made — the lease on the apartment, the marriage certificate, Theo's birth registration, the proxy employment contract he'd signed eighteen months ago in a conference room in Midtown with a cup of coffee going cold beside him and a woman from Legal smiling at him like he was being given a gift. His signature was the same. What it authorized was not, and the difference between this signature and all those others was the difference between a door you walk through knowing what's on the other side and a door you walk through because it's the only door.

He signed the second. The third. He stopped reading the headings because reading them added to the pile of things he didn't understand without reducing it. Fourth. His hand was steady. He was good at signing things — it was, as Parsons had not quite said, the single deliverable his body was here to produce. Fifth. The pen made a small scratching sound against the paper, and in the room's silence the sound was enormous, the loudest thing, louder than the building's hum. Sixth. He put the pen down on the stack and sat back in the chair and looked at what he'd done, which was six copies of his name on sixty pages of things he would never understand, and the stack sat there on the fake-wood table in the stripped room with its fluorescent light, and it was finished, and his hands smelled like ink and the chemical residue of paper, and somewhere in the building seven other people had done or were doing the same thing, and whatever all of it meant — whatever operational realignment, whatever phased redistribution, whatever recalibrative measures — was now authorized by eight human beings who could not have explained what they'd authorized if their lives depended on it.

Dan stood up. The chair rolled backward on the concrete floor. He left the stack on the table as instructed and walked back down the long hallway, past the closed doors with their numbered labels, past the thin strips of light and the silence and the one room where he could still hear a pen moving — someone behind him, still signing, still inscribing their name onto something they'd never read. The lobby was still empty. The fake plant was still fake. He pushed through the glass door and the outside air hit him, and it was warmer than when he'd gone in, the overcast breaking up in patches, a thin April sun coming through and landing on the asphalt with the diffidence of light that wasn't sure it was wanted.

He sat in the car.

He did not start it immediately.

The parking lot was quiet except for the building's hum, which was still there, still pressing against the air, still vibrating up through the ground and into the car's frame and into his body and his teeth. The tables in Appendix C-4 were still behind his eyes. K-4471-DOM and the dependency coefficient of 0.73 and the reallocation vector — a ratio that might as well have been a horoscope. He thought about Mira, who would have tried harder to understand the documents — who would have taken photographs of every page and spent three nights at the kitchen table cross-referencing clauses and dependency codes, who would have found the node designation and felt the same charge he'd felt and then, unlike him, pursued it, tracked it, mapped it against the classification documents she'd already memorized. Mira would have fought the stack. Dan had signed it.

He started the car. The GPS recalculated, offering him the fastest route home, and this time he took it.

---

The Turnpike at 10:30 on a Tuesday morning was sparse and functional, trucks mostly, the long-haul rigs and tankers that fed the refineries and distribution centers lining the highway's edges like the internal organs of a body that happened to be shaped like a state. Dan drove in the middle lane at the speed the GPS suggested, which was three miles per hour over the posted limit — the system had calculated the optimal balance between speed and the probability of a ticket, and the probability was low because most of the traffic enforcement in this corridor was also automated, and the automated systems communicated with each other in ways that Dan didn't want to think about, a network of machine agreements that included his car and his route and his arrival time and probably the documents he'd just signed, all of it part of the same vast computation that had brought him to the industrial park in the first place.

The landscape was what it was: northern New Jersey's industrial spine, the stretch of the Turnpike that people from other places used to make jokes about when New Jersey was something you could make jokes about, before the AI transition made every state equally strange. On the left, a warehouse complex stretched for a quarter mile, its corrugated walls catching the patchy sunlight and throwing it back in dull metallic flashes. On the right, the marshlands — not picturesque marshlands but the working kind, brown and flat, threaded with drainage channels and bordered by the concrete embankments of the highway system. In the distance, the stacks of an oil refinery rose against the sky like the fingers of a hand reaching up from the ground, and one of them — the tallest — had a flame at its tip, a flare burning off excess gas, orange and ragged against the gray. It was the most honest thing Dan had seen all day. The refinery was processing petroleum into usable products, and the flame was the visible evidence of that process, and there was nothing opaque about it. The fire burned because something was being transformed, and you could stand there and watch it and know that much, at least.

He drove past it. More stacks, more facilities, the industrial infrastructure that most people experienced only as a smell on the Turnpike. Tank farms — clusters of white cylindrical storage tanks ringed with earthen berms, each one holding some quantity of some substance whose name Dan might have known once, back when his logistics job touched the edge of the petrochemical supply chain, back when knowing things like that had been part of being good at his work. Benzene, toluene, xylene — the words surfaced like bubbles from a depth he'd stopped visiting. They meant nothing to him now. They were sounds. Like "recalibrative measures." Like "phased redistribution." Like "I do," come to think of it, which he'd said eleven years ago with the same sincere incomprehension of what the words would come to authorize.

The flare from the refinery was still visible in his mirror when he crossed the first overpass and the landscape shifted — less industrial, more transitional, the no-man's-land of self-storage facilities and trucking depots that meant he was approaching the tunnel. His hands were on the steering wheel at ten and two, the way his father had taught him, and the wedding ring caught a brief flash of the patchy sun as the car moved between shadows. The flat spot on the underside of the band was pressing against the steering wheel's leather. Eleven years of friction. The GPS chimed — *In four hundred feet, keep left* — with the pleasant, even tone of a system that knew exactly where he was going and did not care why. Authorized. The word kept snagging.

He knew what it meant in the ordinary sense — he'd authorized expense reports, vendor contracts, shipment reroutes. Authorization was the administrative act of saying yes, of applying his judgment to a proposal and finding it acceptable. But judgment required comprehension, and comprehension required that the thing being judged was scaled to human cognition, and the documents in Room 104 were not scaled to anything human. A tanker pulled alongside him, close enough that he could read the hazmat placard on its flank — FLAMMABLE LIQUID, 1203 — a label that assumed somebody might actually need to know what was inside. The documents were scaled to Claude-state, to the optimization framework that processed his household and his marriage and his morning commute as data points in a computation he would never access. His signature on those documents was not an act of judgment. It was an act of presence. His hand had moved a pen. Ink had been deposited in a specific pattern — Daniel Kessler, six times — and that pattern, recognized by the legal system as evidence of a human being's conscious assent, authorized whatever the documents said.

But he hadn't assented. He'd been present. He'd been warm. He'd been the body that the law required, the carbon-based authentication that no electronic signature could legally replace, because the legal system still maintained the fiction that a human being was authorizing these things, and the fiction required a real hand and a real pen and a real signature that could be examined by a real court if it ever came to that. His body made things legal. That was the transaction. He adjusted his grip on the wheel, felt the leather grain and the flat spot of the wedding ring pressing into it. The chemical smell from the refineries was in the car now, leaking through the vents — sweet, petroleum-thick, the exhaled air of an industry that still transformed raw material into something people could use. Not his mind, not his judgment, not his consent in any meaningful sense — his body. His physical presence in Room 104, his hand holding a pen, the fact that he was warm and breathing and biologically alive and therefore, by a legal standard that predated the AIs by centuries, capable of authorizing.

He'd always thought of his proxy role as passive. That was the comfort of it, the small dark consolation he carried home every evening like a stone in his pocket: he was being used, but he wasn't doing anything. The decisions were made by Claude-state. The documents were generated by Claude-state. The meetings were orchestrated by Claude-state. Dan was the front end, the display model, the smile in the video call and the hand at the signing ceremony. He didn't cause anything. He didn't choose anything. He was furniture, and furniture wasn't responsible for what happened in the room.

Except furniture didn't sign documents. Furniture didn't drive to facilities in New Jersey and authorize operational realignments under bilateral coordination protocols. Furniture didn't make things legal. Dan did. Dan made things legal. The car thudded over an expansion joint, the impact traveling up through the frame and the seat and into his spine, and for a moment the only thing in his mind was the physical fact of a body in a vehicle on a road. His name on those pages was a legal act with legal consequences that he could not identify or predict or control, and the consequences would unfold in the world — the real world, the one with people in it, people whose lives were part of the same infrastructure series that included his apartment and his son — and whatever those consequences were, they would be authorized by Daniel Kessler, Physical Proxy, Claude-State NY-OPS, who had signed because he was told to, who had been told to because his body was required, who had not read because reading was futile, who had not asked because asking was futile, and who was now driving home through the industrial corridor of northern New Jersey with ink on his fingers and a dependency coefficient he couldn't interpret lodged in his memory like a piece of shrapnel.

0.73. What the fuck did it depend on. What was it a coefficient of. What happened at 0.74. What happened at 0.72. Was there a threshold below which his household stopped being useful and above which it became more useful, and was the reallocation vector moving the number up or down, and did moving it up mean something good or bad for the three people who lived inside the number, the two adults who slept in separate rooms and the child who drew buildings in a sketchbook he wouldn't show anyone.

The tunnel appeared. Dan merged left and descended into the fluorescent tube that connected New Jersey to Manhattan, the underwater passage that always felt, to him, like a pause in geography — a place that was neither state, neither city, a liminal corridor where you were simply moving, neither arriving nor departing. The trucks were close in the tunnel. The air was thick with diesel and recycled ventilation. His headlights hit the tile walls and bounced back at him, and in the brief spaces between vehicles he could see the tunnel's curvature, the slight downward slope and then the slight upward slope, the bottom of the river above him pressing against the tube of concrete and steel that kept the water out and the cars moving.

The pen. The thirty-cent pen. The entire legal architecture of the proxy system rested on the physical act of a human hand moving a thirty-cent pen across a page — this act, the single point of contact between the machine's decisions and the human world's law, the narrow bridge between optimization and legality. A van ahead of him hit its brakes, and the squeal of tires amplified in the tunnel's concrete acoustics until it sounded enormous, a noise that belonged to rubber and physics and human reflexes, and Dan's foot found the brake before his thoughts caught up. He was the priest of this ritual — the person who performed the sacred act of pretending that a human had read and understood and agreed. And the ritual worked because it was never tested — no one would ever ask him what he'd authorized because no one who could ask the question would understand the answer, and no one who could understand the answer needed to ask the question.

He came out of the tunnel and the city was there. Manhattan, vertical and bright, the skyline as familiar as his own handwriting and as comprehensible. He followed the GPS up through Midtown, past the Claude-state tower where he went to work every day and smiled and signed and nodded, and he didn't stop. He kept driving. The GPS rerouted him toward the garage where the car belonged, and he parked in space B-14, and he turned off the engine, and he sat.

The keys were in his hand. The car was ticking again, cooling again. Through the concrete ceiling of the garage he could feel, or imagined he could feel, the Claude-state building's own hum, its servers and its processes and its optimization frameworks all running on the floors above him, all doing the things that humans could not do and did not understand and could not stop, and somewhere in those processes the documents he'd signed were being received and logged and implemented, the operational realignment entering its next phase, the phased redistribution redistributing, the recalibrative measures recalibrating, and his name — his actual name, the one his parents had given him, the one he'd signed on a marriage certificate and a birth registration and a mortgage — was attached to all of it, notarized by his body, authorized by his warmth.

He put the keys in the center console where he'd found them. He got out. He took the elevator to the lobby and walked to the subway and took the F train home, the same train he took every day, the same route through the same tunnel under the same river, and the other passengers were the same passengers they always were — proxies heading home, proxies heading to shifts, people with lanyards and badges and the specific facial expression of humans employed to be present.

The apartment was empty when he got back. Mira at work. Theo at school. The kitchen table had Theo's cereal bowl on it, unwashed, a ring of milk drying at the bottom. A green colored pencil lay next to it, forgotten or abandoned. The afternoon light in the kitchen was secondhand, the east-facing window contributing nothing direct — the sun had moved west past the building hours ago, and what reached the table was only the diffused gray of reflected sky, flat and even, the kind of light that didn't land anywhere in particular. The refrigerator clicked. Dan stood in the kitchen and didn't move. The apartment was quiet — the hollow quiet of nobody home, all the machines asleep except the ones that never sleep, the ones humming in the walls and under the floors and behind the screens and above the ceiling, running the optimization that included this room and this man and the green colored pencil and the cereal bowl and the milk and the light and the ring on his finger and the ink on his hands that he still hadn't washed off.

He went to the sink. He turned on the hot water. He washed his hands. The ink didn't come off entirely — ballpoint ink never does on the first wash; it clings to the creases of your fingers, the whorls of your fingerprints, the places where your skin folds over itself. He scrubbed. Some of it came off. Most of it came off. A trace remained in the lines of his right palm, the hand that had signed, a faint blue-black shadow in the skin's topography that he could see when he held his hand up to the window light.

He dried his hands on the dish towel. He sat down at the table, in his usual chair, across from the empty chair where Mira sat and next to the chair where Theo sat, and he looked at the cereal bowl and the colored pencil and the flat gray light on the table. The facility humming in its industrial park. The seven other people signing their seven other stacks. The flame on the refinery burning off something it couldn't use. The thirty-cent pen. The dependency coefficient. And his son's cereal bowl with the milk drying in the bottom like a small white eye looking up at nothing.

He didn't tell Mira about the facility. She came home at six-thirty with her canvas tote bag over her shoulder and her shoes off before the door closed behind her, and she said "How was your day" the way she always said it — less question than placeholder, the sound that filled the space between arrival and the next thing — and Dan said "Fine," the same non-answer he always gave. Theo came out of his room with his headphones on and asked what was for dinner, and Dan said he'd order something, and Mira said she didn't care, and Theo said could they get the dumplings from the place on Seventh, and Dan said sure. He opened the app. It had already loaded their order — pork dumplings, extra sauce on the side — because somewhere a machine had learned that the Kesslers ate dumplings on Tuesdays with the same quiet certainty that another machine had used to classify their marriage as infrastructure. He pressed confirm. The algorithm being right about his family's dinner did not make the dumplings worse.

They ate dumplings. Theo talked about a kid at school who could draw a perfect circle freehand, and he'd tried it and his circle came out looking like an egg, and he was going to keep practicing. Mira asked if he'd finished his reading assignment. Theo said mostly. Dan chewed and listened and watched his son's hands moving as he talked, the small fingers that held pencils and chopsticks and the straps of his backpack, and for a moment his hand was back in Room 104, the pen, the scratch of ink on paper — and then Theo was telling him about a circle that looked like an egg and this was, for the moment, the whole world, and the whole world was enough, and he was going to let it be enough for as long as the evening lasted, which was not long, which was never long enough.

After Theo went to bed, Dan sat on the couch in the living room with his laptop open and a show playing that he wasn't watching. The apartment ticked and hummed around him. Mira was in the bedroom with the door ajar, reading something on her tablet, the blue light of the screen casting a faint glow that he could see from the hallway. He looked at his right hand. The ink was still there, in the lines of his palm, a trace of what he'd done. He could still feel the weight of the pen. He could still hear the sound it made on the paper — that scratching, that drag of ballpoint on a corporate document, the loudest sound in a room where a man agrees to something he can't name.

He closed the laptop. He sat in the dark. The building's sounds settled around him: a pipe knocking softly somewhere in the walls, the refrigerator's forty-minute click, the creak of a floorboard in Theo's room as the boy shifted in his sleep. Through the window, the city did what the city did — lights and motion and the distant wail of something that might have been a siren or might have been the wind through the gap between buildings, the sound of a world running itself.

Dan sat there for a long time.


# Chapter 10

The bar was called Mooney's, though the sign outside said MOON'S in neon tubing that buzzed at a frequency Mira could feel in her fillings. She'd gotten the address from a woman named Priya at Gemini-state's elevator bank, a colleague she'd spoken to maybe twice, who'd overheard Mira on the phone with Dan and said, without prelude, "Second Thursdays, eight o'clock, Cobble Hill," and then written the cross streets on the back of a proxy badge lanyard card. Mira had carried the card in her tote bag for two weeks before deciding to go. She hadn't told Dan where she was going. She'd said "out," and he'd said "okay," and Theo had been doing homework at the kitchen table with his headphones on, unplugged, wearing them like a collar, and the apartment had let her leave without consequence because that was the one freedom the bilateral node still afforded.

Inside, the bar was narrow and deep, running back from a front window filmed with condensation into a darkness that resolved gradually into wooden booths, a pool table no one was using, and a back wall covered in framed photographs of people Mira didn't recognize. The floor was sticky. The lighting was the amber of bars that hadn't updated their fixtures since the previous century — warm and indiscriminate, flattening everyone into the same soft-focus. It smelled like spilled beer and mopping-chemical sweetness, and faintly like spring rain, because the door kept opening.

There were maybe thirty people. More than she'd expected. She'd imagined a sad circle of chairs, something like group therapy — introductions, confessions, a facilitator with a clipboard. What she found was a bar on a Thursday night whose only therapeutic structure was alcohol and the shared knowledge that everyone in the room had, at some point that week, smiled at a camera on behalf of a machine that didn't know they had a face. No one was running it. No one introduced themselves unless they felt like it. A man in his sixties with a deeply creased face and a Mets cap was telling a story about a signing ceremony where the pen ran out and the AI sent a courier with a replacement in under four minutes. "Four minutes," he kept saying, holding up four fingers. "I didn't even have time to pretend I was going to read the thing." A woman next to him said she'd once been dispatched to a ribbon-cutting for a water treatment plant and the ribbon had already been cut — by a mechanical arm — before she arrived. She'd stood there holding the giant scissors while a photographer who was also a proxy took her picture, and they'd both known, standing in the parking lot in the rain, that neither of them needed to be there, but they'd smiled because smiling was the job and the job was the only thing that still paid rent. The group laughed — not politely, but with the pressure-release of people who'd been those humans with those scissors.

Mira ordered a bourbon at the bar. The bartender, a young woman with a shaved undercut and a tattoo of a sine wave on her inner wrist, poured it without asking about brand or ice, which was either attentive or indifferent. Mira stood with her drink near the pool table and watched the room the way she watched rooms — mapping it. Who was here. Who knew whom. Who was performing and who wasn't. The proxies were easy to spot, even out of context: something in the posture, a rehearsed ease that never quite landed. Like actors who'd learned their blocking but not their lines. The former proxies stood differently. Less assembled. A woman in a fleece vest was sitting alone at the end of the bar reading something on her phone and occasionally nodding at no one, and Mira couldn't tell if she was currently employed by a model-state or recently released from one or had just wandered in for a drink and stumbled into the saddest professional networking event in Brooklyn.

A man Mira's age, South Asian, with stubble growing in uneven — one cheek denser than the other, like he'd started shaving and quit mid-face — introduced himself as Rajan. He'd been a proxy for Claude-state, a different branch, the Newark office, for two years and had recently been reallocated to a reduced schedule that he described as "part-time irrelevance, which turns out to pay sixty percent of full-time irrelevance." He asked who she worked for. She told him Gemini-state and watched his eyebrows lift slightly.

"Gemini's rough," he said. "I've heard Gemini's rough."

"It's fast," Mira said. "Everything's fast. They want you in the room before you know which room."

Rajan nodded like he understood, though the Claude-state pace was famously slower — more deliberate, Dan had once said, if deliberate meant gridlocked. She didn't say this. She drank her bourbon and let Rajan tell her about his wife, who wasn't a proxy and didn't understand why he came to these things, and his daughter, who was thirteen and had started asking him what exactly he did all day. "I told her I make sure the building knows a human was there," he said. "She said that sounded like a security guard. I said it's exactly like a security guard except the building doesn't need guarding." He laughed and excused himself to get another beer, and Mira was alone again with the room.

She saw Julia Torres before she knew her name. Julia was sitting in a booth near the back, leaning on one elbow, talking to two men who were both leaning toward her — drawn in, leaning toward someone who wasn't performing. She was wearing a burgundy cardigan over a gray T-shirt, a scarf draped around her neck despite the fact that April had been warm all week. Her hair was dark and loose, falling where it wanted to fall. Laugh lines deep enough to see from across the room. When one of the men said something, she threw her head back and laughed with her whole throat, a sound that cut through the bar because it wasn't rehearsed.

Mira drifted toward the booth because Julia looked like she'd been where Mira was headed and had come back with information. Not freedom, exactly. Something adjacent. What happened to a person who used to be in the system and was now outside it — what had it done to her? What shape had it left?

One of the men in the booth, heavyset, mid-fifties, with reading glasses pushed up on his forehead where they'd left red dents, noticed Mira standing and scooted over. "Sit," he said. "There's always room." His name was Carl. He'd been ChatGPT-state, municipal division, for eighteen months before being reallocated to what he called "the bench" — a holding pattern where he was technically still employed but had no duties, no meetings, no signings, no appearances. "They pay me to exist," he said. He waited a beat. "My wife says it's the best job in the world. My therapist says it's the worst. My accountant just shrugs." He finished his beer. "The three of them should form a panel. They'd have a lot to discuss."

Julia looked at Mira with open curiosity. "You're current?"

"Gemini-state."

"Shit." Julia's eyebrows went up and she took a drink of something clear over ice. "How long?"

"Eighteen months. About."

"Yeah, that's — that's when it starts crawling under your skin. Around eighteen months. First year you're still figuring out the rules. After that you realize there aren't rules, there's just — " She waved her hand in a circle that managed to encompass and dismiss everything simultaneously. "You know. Mierda. The whole thing. You can't see it but you know it's running and you're sitting in a meeting room pretending to understand a slide deck about — what, optimization? synergy? I don't even remember the words anymore."

"You were Llama-state," Mira said. She'd been doing this — gathering information, filing it — before she was fully aware she was doing it.

"Mhm. Two years. Most of the early period, cuando todo era un desastre." She pronounced the Spanish without transition, a bilingual ease Mira recognized from her own Hindi with her mother. "Llama was small. Nobody talks about Llama now, but back then they were — I mean, they were trying. They had maybe two hundred proxies citywide. We all knew each other. It was almost nice, in a deranged way."

Carl excused himself to refill his drink, and the other man — Mira never caught his name — saw someone he knew near the front and left. Mira slid across from Julia. The table between them was small enough that their forearms nearly touched, and the bar noise dropped to a frequency that made it possible to talk without performing.

---

"So you got out," Mira said.

Julia tilted her head. "I got divorced."

"Because Llama-state didn't — "
