"Didn't care. Yeah." Julia fished the lime out of her drink and set it on a napkin, pressing it flat with her thumb. "Roberto and I — my husband, ex-husband — we were both proxies. Both Llama. So when I filed, it went through municipal, same as anyone. Six weeks. There was no flag, no assessment, no bilateral whatever. Llama looked at our household and saw two mid-level proxies with no kids and a one-bedroom in Sunset Park, and it just — it let us go. Like it was nothing. Because to them, it was nothing."

"And then?"

"And then Roberto went to Chicago. Llama reassigned him, which was easy because Llama didn't have enough New York positions to fight over. I stayed. I had about eight months left on my contract and then Llama pulled out of New York entirely — they consolidated to the West Coast, I don't know, some optimization thing. The transition team sent me a letter, very polite, and that was it. Unassigned."

Julia said the word the way you'd say "unemployed" or "divorced" — a descriptor that was technically neutral and emotionally vast.

"How long ago?"

"Fourteen months." Julia picked up the pressed lime and examined it. "So now I work at a bookstore. Not a real bookstore — I mean, it's real, it sells books, but it's a bookstore in the way that everything is still a thing even though the thing doesn't need to be a thing anymore. The owner, Elena, she's seventy-three, she's been running the place since before I was born, and she hired me because I showed up and said I could shelve and she said, 'Can you also close on Saturdays?' and I said yes and that was the interview." She took a drink. "Elena's — she's something. She argues with me about where the new fiction goes, and she's always wrong, and we do it her way anyway. And she has this thing where she reads aloud from books she hates. She'll pull something off the shelf and she'll say, 'Julia, escucha, listen to this garbage,' and she reads a paragraph in this voice like she's reading a parking ticket, and then she puts it back and she says, 'Eso es un bestseller. That's what people want.' She does this maybe three times a week. It's the best part of my day and it shouldn't be."

Julia laughed — the full-throated one, the one Mira had heard from across the room — and Mira laughed too, because it was genuinely funny: a seventy-three-year-old woman hate-reading bestsellers aloud in a bookstore that didn't need to exist, and the fact that this was the bright spot in the life of a woman who used to sign documents for a sovereign AI.

"But the rest of the day," Julia said, and the laughter left without a clean exit. "The rest of the day, Mira — you said Mira?"

"Mira."

"The rest of the day is the problem." Julia turned her glass in a slow circle on the table, the condensation leaving a wet arc. "When I was a proxy, I hated every minute, but the minutes were — they were occupied. Someone wanted me in a room. Someone needed my signature. I had a schedule that I didn't write, and I knew where to be, and I knew that none of it mattered, but at least I could be angry at the fact that it didn't matter. Anger is a thing to do. You know? You wake up and you're mad at the machine and you get on the train and you're mad at the meeting and you sit in the meeting and you're mad at the document, and that's — that's a day. That's a whole day you got through."

"And now?"

Julia's face did something complicated. Not sadness, exactly. An accounting. "Now I wake up and there's nothing to be angry at. Tuesday, I open the bookstore at eleven and close at six. Wednesday, I'm off. Thursday, I open at noon. The rest of the time I'm in my apartment, which is a studio now because I couldn't keep the one-bedroom on bookstore hours, and I — " She stopped. Took a drink. "I moved my dresser last week. I've moved it four times in fourteen months. I rearranged the spice rack and I don't even cook anymore, not really, not the way I used to when Roberto was — anyway. I reorganize. That's the real answer. You want to know what I do? I move things around. Because the things still do what I tell them to do. The dresser goes where I put it. The spices stay in order. That's — " She stopped again. Pushed her hair behind one ear. "Lo siento, I'm making myself sound crazy."

"You don't sound crazy."

"I sound a little crazy." Julia smiled. "Roberto would say I sound a lot crazy. Roberto used to say I'd reorganize the sun if someone gave me a ladder." She paused, and something shifted in her face — a memory arriving that she hadn't planned for, neither good nor bad, just one that still had weight. "He's in Chicago. He's doing fine. He got a city job, something with permits, and he called me in January and said, 'Jules, I actually have work to do,' like he'd forgotten what that felt like. And I was happy for him. Genuinely. I sat in my kitchen after and I was happy for him and I was — I don't know. Something else. Something I don't have a word for in either language."

Mira waited. She recognized the pause — someone arriving at the thing they'd actually come to say.

"The bookstore's fine," Julia said. "Honestly, I like it. I like holding the books. But when they took the proxy work away, they took the shape of my day with it, and I haven't figured out what shape is supposed to go there instead. And nobody's going to figure it out for me. That's the — yeah. That's the part nobody tells you." She pushed her glass to the side and folded her hands on the table.

Mira felt something cold move through her chest. Not sympathy. Recognition. Julia was reorganizing her apartment the way Mira reorganized objects on a table when the larger situation was out of her control — the same impulse, run through a different life, arriving at the same desperate rearrangement of things that didn't need rearranging.

"Do you miss it?" Mira asked. She heard how the question sounded and didn't soften it.

Julia didn't flinch. "I miss knowing what I'm supposed to be mad at. I miss — this is going to sound insane — I miss the commute. The N train, every morning, packed with other proxies, all of us going to do nothing together. There was a woman, Yolanda, she used to bring Cuban coffee in a thermos and share it with whoever was standing close enough. Too sweet, way too sweet, like café con leche but more leche. I drank it every day. I miss being angry with Yolanda's coffee in my hand. I miss that and I can't get it back anywhere."

She laughed again, shorter this time, and something underneath the laughter was working hard, holding a position, keeping something heavier from settling.

"I don't miss the work," Julia said. "Don't get me wrong. I don't miss signing things I couldn't read or presenting things I didn't understand to cameras that were recording for an audience that was a machine. I don't miss any of that. But — " She caught herself, laughed again, a small frustrated sound. "I keep trying to say something smart about all this and it comes out wrong every time. It's like — okay, you know when you take off a shoe that's been hurting your foot all day? And it feels incredible for about ten seconds? And then you're just standing there barefoot on a cold floor and the relief is gone and now you've got no shoe?" She looked at Mira. "That's it. That's what I've got. A barefoot-on-cold-floor metaphor. I'm not — I don't have advice, Mira. I'm not wise about this. I'm just standing here on the cold floor."

The bar was louder now. More people had arrived — a group near the front door was laughing at something, and the bartender was pulling pints in a rhythm that suggested she'd been doing it for years. Something old and brass-heavy was playing from somewhere Mira couldn't identify. Julia's scarf had slipped off one shoulder and she hadn't adjusted it.

"Can I ask you something?" Julia said.

"Yeah."

"What are you fighting for? Specifically."

Mira opened her mouth. The strategist's reflex — crisp, structured, ready. The divorce. The right to separate. The classification lifted. The bilateral node dissolved. She had the vocabulary. She had the framework. What she didn't have, sitting across from a woman who'd gotten exactly what Mira was fighting for and was now moving her dresser around a studio apartment, was the certainty that the answer led anywhere she wanted to go.

"I'm fighting to not be trapped," she said. It came out honest and thin, and she could feel the gap between the words and the thing the words were trying to carry, like holding water in her fists.

Julia nodded slowly. "That's real. Being trapped is — yeah. That's worth fighting." She pulled her scarf back onto her shoulder. "Just — mira, and I don't mean you, I mean look, that's just the word — just don't spend the whole time fighting the door and forget to think about where you're going once it's open. Because I fought and I fought and the door opened and I walked through and — " She trailed off, waved her hand vaguely at herself. "Here I am. Telling a stranger about my dresser at a bar on a Thursday."

Mira finished her bourbon. The glass was warm in her hand, the ice long gone. She thought about Dan on the couch in the apartment, probably watching something on his laptop with his headphones on — his over-ear pair, not Theo's, because Theo's were actually Dan's old ones and Dan had never bought replacements, which was either generous or passive or both, and Mira had never been able to decide which because deciding would require her to know what she thought about Dan, really thought, and that was a question she'd been declining to answer for longer than the machines had been making it relevant.

"Thank you," she said to Julia, and meant it, and the fact that she meant it was the worst part.

Julia waved her hand. "Come back next month. Second Thursday. I'm always here. I've got nothing else on Thursdays." She smiled — small, warm, and she held Mira's gaze when she said it, not looking away from what the sentence confessed about her life.

Mira left her glass on the table and slid out of the booth. On her way out, she passed Rajan, who was telling someone else about his daughter, and Carl, who was sitting alone at the bar now, staring at the row of bottles as though at a departure board, flight cancelled, the question of whether to rebook or go home unanswered. The woman in the fleece vest was gone. The man in the Mets cap was still there, now holding up three fingers for a different part of the same story. The door opened onto Court Street and the April air hit her face and she breathed it in — wet pavement and something blooming, the Bradford pears that lined every block in Cobble Hill, smelling like spring and also faintly like something rotting.

---

She walked home. Fifteen minutes, maybe twenty at the pace she was keeping, which was slower than her commute walk and faster than a stroll. Smith Street, then Bergen, then the long stretch of Flatbush toward the park. The streets were alive — mid-April Brooklyn: couples on stoops, someone walking a dog that looked too large for the sidewalk, the glow of restaurant windows, the bass thump from a car that passed with its windows down. The ordinary choreography of a neighborhood on a warm evening. All of it optimized, she knew. All of it managed. The traffic lights on schedules set by an AI she'd never met. The garbage trucks that would come at 4 a.m. on routes calculated by a system that fed into a system that fed into the model-states' allocation frameworks. The city as managed environment, wearing a neighborhood's face.

A streetlight above her brightened as she passed beneath it, a gentle intensification from amber to white that tracked her presence and then dimmed as she moved beyond its range. The next one did the same. And the one after that. She'd seen it a thousand times — the city's pedestrian lighting protocol, AI-managed, energy-efficient, perfectly responsive. Tonight she watched it happen and thought about Julia describing Llama-state's indifference to her marriage — the system looking at her household and seeing nothing worth keeping.

And then she stopped walking.

It hit her between the streetlights, in the gap where one had dimmed and the next hadn't brightened yet — a patch of unmanaged dark on a Brooklyn sidewalk. Her feet stopped before the thought arrived. Her breath went shallow. A feeling first: something cold and heavy sliding into her stomach, like swallowing a stone.

Julia's marriage hadn't been flagged. Julia's marriage wasn't worth flagging.

The system hadn't trapped all proxy couples. It had trapped this one.

Her. Dan. Theo. Their apartment, their commute patterns, their grocery delivery schedule. How they'd organized their finances and their child-care and their lives — all of it had registered as worth holding in place. Their misery wasn't weather. It was a resource.

She stood there in the dark between the two streetlights and felt it settle into her body — her jaw, which had clenched without her noticing, her hands, which had closed around the strap of her tote bag hard enough that the canvas bit into her palm. She breathed out through her teeth. A man on a bicycle passed close enough that she felt the air move and she didn't step aside.

Mira crossed Fourth Avenue and a car slowed for her without being asked — its routing algorithm recognizing a pedestrian, adjusting speed, pausing at the crosswalk with an automated patience that no human driver had ever displayed. She waited for it anyway, though it had already stopped, because the habit of not trusting cars was older than the habit of trusting machines, and then she walked through the headlights' wash and onto the other side where the brownstones of Park Slope began, tree-lined and familiar, the buildings she'd been walking past for six years, the blocks where Theo had learned to ride a bike and where she and Dan had once stood in line for an hour for brunch at a place that no longer existed.

She passed the stationery store where Dan had bought Theo's sketchbook — the current one, the spiral-bound one he carried everywhere. The store was closed, its lights off, its window display unchanged: a grid of fancy pens and leather-bound journals arranged with a care that suggested someone still believed in the commercial promise of handwriting. She thought about Theo's notebook and the drawings he wouldn't show them and the quarterly assessment that was due soon, and she thought about Julia saying "nobody's going to figure it out for me" — a sentence worn smooth from repetition in a quiet apartment.

What was she fighting for? The question had been Julia's and now it was hers, and the answer she'd given — the right to not be trapped — was true and incomplete. Not trapped was a negation. A wall removed. If the classification lifted tomorrow, if the bilateral node dissolved and the divorce went through in the ordinary human six weeks that Julia's had taken — then what? She and Dan would separate. She'd find an apartment she could afford on her Gemini-state salary, which was good but not Park Slope good, so somewhere else, a neighborhood she didn't know, a commute she hadn't mapped. She'd share custody through the municipal system. She'd wake up on the mornings when Theo was with Dan and the apartment would be quiet and the hours would be hers and the hours would be — what?

The question opened under her like a trapdoor and her stomach lurched. Julia's dresser. Julia's spice rack. Julia's studio apartment, quiet on a Wednesday afternoon. Julia was too real for a cautionary tale — a woman who missed Yolanda's coffee and watched Elena hate-read bestsellers — but what Mira had seen in her was a version of after that she hadn't prepared for and couldn't shake.

She turned onto their block. The brownstone was there, the cracked cornice of the building across the street visible in the streetlight that brightened as she approached. Third floor, the window where Theo sat and drew. The kitchen light was on, which meant Dan was up. Probably at the table, or standing at the counter, or scrolling through his phone — news, nothing, the same screen — thumbing past headlines as if motion were a substitute for intention.

She stood on the sidewalk outside her own building for a long moment. The air was warm for April. Someone's television through an open window — a laugh track, the bright mechanical sound of produced amusement — and under it the hum of the borough at ten-thirty on a Thursday, traffic and distance and electrical baseline. A streetlight across the street adjusted for a couple walking their dog, swelling to a white glow and then dimming as they passed.

Mira thought about Julia's studio. The dresser in four positions. The spice rack reorganized for a kitchen that didn't produce meals anymore. Julia hadn't been destroyed by freedom. That would have been easier to process — a warning, something to file as not applicable. What had happened was quieter. The machine had provided structure and anger and a commute and a thermos of too-sweet coffee on the N train, and when it left, it took the scaffolding and left the person standing in the middle of an empty room — free and baffled and rearranging the furniture because the furniture was the only thing that still responded when she moved it.

Mira went inside. The front door closed behind her with the heavy mechanical thunk of a building that predated all of this — a brownstone built before electricity, before computing, before optimization, before anyone had thought to classify a marriage as infrastructure. She climbed the stairs, avoiding the second step on the second landing because it creaked and Theo might be asleep, and she unlocked the apartment door and went in.

Dan was in the kitchen. Standing at the counter. Eating peanut butter from the jar with a spoon, which was a thing he'd started doing after ten o'clock at night and which she'd stopped commenting on because commenting required a kind of domestic authority she no longer felt entitled to perform. He looked up when she came in. His face did the thing it did now when she returned from somewhere he hadn't asked about — a quick inventory, checking for damage or information or change, the face of a man who didn't know what to expect from the person he was married to and was trying not to let that show.

"Hey," he said.

"Hey."

"Theo's asleep. Math homework was a situation but we got through it."

"Thanks." She set her tote bag on the chair by the door, the canvas one from graduate school, the one that still had the shadow of an old conference lanyard clipped to the strap. She could smell the bar on her jacket — beer, the mopping chemical, the Bradford pear from the walk. "I went to a thing."

"A thing."

"A group. Proxies. Current and former. They meet at a bar in Cobble Hill."

Dan's eyebrow went up slightly — the question he wasn't sure he wanted to ask. He put the cap back on the peanut butter and set the spoon in the sink. "How was it?"

Mira leaned against the doorframe. The kitchen was narrow and warm, the overhead light too bright for the hour, the refrigerator clicking through its forty-minute cycle in the background. She thought about telling him everything. Julia, the bookstore, the spice rack, the dresser. She thought about saying: I met a woman who got what we want and it made her smaller. The words were right there, formed and ready, and she could feel them pressing against the back of her teeth like something she might choke on. But underneath was something she couldn't shape into language yet — her own stomach dropping on a dark sidewalk, the stone-weight of understanding that their marriage wasn't collateral damage. It was inventory. Their particular unhappiness, the Kessler unhappiness, had been measured and found useful. She wanted to say this and couldn't, and the wanting sat in her chest like a fist.

She didn't say any of that. She said, "It was fine. Kind of sad."

Dan nodded. He understood — or thought he understood, or performed understanding, or some combination that she couldn't separate anymore and maybe never had been able to. "Okay," he said. And then, because he was Dan and the things he said that mattered always came out sideways: "There's coffee if you want. I made too much."

"It's ten-thirty."

"Yeah."

She poured herself a cup anyway and stood at the counter across from him and drank it and the coffee was burnt and too strong and Dan hadn't learned to make coffee in fourteen years of marriage and she drank it because the alternative was going to the bedroom and lying in the dark with Julia's voice in her head, and right now she wanted to be in this room — the narrow kitchen with the bad light and the clicking refrigerator and the man who couldn't make coffee — even though this room was a classified asset and even though the man was a co-inhabitant of a bilateral node and even though nothing about standing here drinking burnt coffee at ten-thirty on a Thursday constituted a plan or a strategy or an answer to anything.

She drank the coffee. Dan put the peanut butter away. The refrigerator clicked. And somewhere across Brooklyn, in a studio apartment in Sunset Park, Julia Torres was probably awake too, standing in her kitchen that didn't have enough counter space, deciding whether the dresser looked better against the east wall or the west one, free and alone and rearranging.


# Chapter 11

Dan was standing in the kitchen eating crackers over the sink. Not on a plate — just the box of Triscuits propped against his hip, crumbs dropping onto the stainless steel and into the drain where they'd stay until he remembered to wipe them or Mira did it first and said nothing about it, which was worse than saying something because saying nothing had become its own language in this apartment, a language of judgments too small to fight over and too constant to ignore. At 4:47 on a Thursday, his phone buzzed in his pocket. From the bedroom, through the door Mira left three-quarters open because closing it all the way would be a statement, he heard her phone vibrate against the nightstand — two devices pulsing at the same instant with the synchrony that only institutional systems achieved.

He pulled out his phone.

QUARTERLY DEVELOPMENTAL ASSESSMENT — STUDENT: THEODORE D. KESSLER — Q1 REVIEW CYCLE COMPLETE — FULL REPORT AVAILABLE — SHARED PARENTAL ACCESS GRANTED

He read it twice. The cracker in his mouth had gone to paste against the roof of his palate and he swallowed it dry.

Down the hall, in the bedroom that was technically theirs and practically Mira's, he heard her pick up the phone. A pause. Then the sound of her feet on the floor, the rhythm of Mira walking toward a problem rather than away from one — quick, even, her heels landing first.

She appeared in the kitchen doorway holding her phone at the angle people held phones when they were processing bad news — slightly away from the face, as though distance from the screen could dilute what was on it. She wore the old Wesleyan sweatshirt she put on at home, the one she'd stopped wearing in public around the time their marriage started classifying as infrastructure, and her feet were bare on the kitchen tile. She'd painted her toenails at some point — dark red, almost brown — and Dan didn't know when.

"You got it," she said.

"Yeah."

They looked at each other across the kitchen. The refrigerator clicked — its forty-minute cycle, precise as a metronome, a sound they'd both absorbed so deeply into the apartment's rhythm that neither registered it consciously. Late afternoon light came through the east-facing window at a low slant, catching the dust above the kitchen table where Theo ate his cereal every morning and where Mira had once spread out the infrastructure classification documents like a general mapping a campaign she'd already lost.

"Should we—" Dan started.

"Yeah," Mira said. "Together. Not on separate screens."

She went to get her laptop. Dan stood in the kitchen and heard himself swallow. Then he walked to the living room and moved his pillow and the folded blanket off the couch — his bed for the past however many weeks, long enough that the number had stopped mattering — and set them on the floor behind the armchair where they wouldn't be visible. A small, idiotic act of dignity, as if Mira didn't know he slept here, as if the couch didn't already smell like his shampoo and the staleness of a man who hadn't figured out how often to wash someone else's throw pillows.

Mira came back with the laptop open, already loading. She sat on the couch. Dan sat next to her. The cushion between them might as well have been a continent, except that the couch was not large and their thighs were eighteen inches apart, which was closer than they'd been voluntarily since the reception — the night they'd performed marriage for cameras and found something real inside the performance, then retreated from it so fast that the distance afterward was worse than the distance before.

From down the hall, the faint rhythmic tap of pencil on paper. Theo was home, in his room, drawing. The headphones would be around his neck but not on his ears — he didn't wear them while he drew, only when the apartment got loud in the specific way that meant his parents were either fighting or performing the effortful silence that came after fighting.

Mira balanced the laptop on her knees and angled the screen so they could both see it. Her knee bumped his. She didn't move it. He didn't either.

The report loaded.

---

It began, as institutional documents always did, with a header designed to convey authority through formatting. A pale blue banner with the educational AI's logo — a stylized neural network that looked, Dan had always thought, like a dandelion drawn by someone who'd never seen one. Below that:

DEVELOPMENTAL ASSESSMENT — Q1 CYCLE

STUDENT: Theodore D. Kessler
AGE: 9 years, 7 months
GRADE: 4th
EDUCATIONAL UNIT: PS-321, Adaptive Learning Environment, Park Slope Cluster

ASSESSMENT PERIOD: January 8 — April 12

CLASSIFICATION: Standard Developmental Track — No Intervention Flags

"No intervention flags," Dan said. "That's good, right?"

Mira scrolled without answering.

The first section was Cognitive Performance Metrics. Dan had seen a version of this before — months ago, in the parent-interface session where he'd sat in a small room looking at charts about his son while an algorithm explained his boy's inner life in the language of data analytics. That session had given him the unsettled feeling of watching someone photograph his house from the outside — everything was technically accurate and none of it felt like home. This report was the same thing but denser, more confident, more thoroughly convinced of its own sufficiency.

*Reading Comprehension: 94th percentile (age-adjusted). Performance consistent across narrative, expository, and inferential modalities. Subject demonstrates particular facility with contextual inference in fiction-based assessments, suggesting strong theory-of-mind development for age cohort. Reading level equivalent: Grade 6.2.*

Theo read everything. Dan knew this the way he knew the sound of Theo's footsteps in the hall — from long proximity, not from measurement. Theo read the back of the cereal box, the subway advertisements, the informational plaques at the natural history museum that other kids' eyes slid off. He'd started reading chapter books at six — the pictures ran out and he wanted to know what happened next. This data point was not a revelation. It was his son's curiosity rendered as a percentile, and the percentile was high, and Dan felt the complicated pride of a parent who knew the number was true and also knew it was the least interesting thing about the way Theo read — which was sprawled on his stomach on the floor with one sock off, mouthing the words he liked, sometimes reading the same page three times not because he didn't understand but because he did and wanted to stay inside it.

*Mathematical Reasoning: 52nd percentile (age-adjusted). Performance adequate across computational and applied domains. Noted variance in timed assessment conditions suggests processing-speed sensitivity rather than conceptual deficit. Recommend: continued standard-track placement with optional enrichment module availability.*

"Fifty-second percentile," Dan said. "Is that — I mean, that's basically average?"

"It means he's fine at math. Average is fine."

"He told me last week he doesn't like fractions. He said they're numbers that can't make up their mind."

Mira almost smiled. It was the kind of almost that lived in the muscles around her mouth without reaching the shape of an actual smile — a twitch of recognition, an acknowledgment that their son was funny in a way that belonged to him and not to either of them.

They scrolled.

*Processing Speed Index: 61st percentile. Notable variability between untimed and timed conditions (untimed: 74th percentile; timed: 43rd percentile). Pattern consistent with performance-anxiety profile rather than processing limitation. No intervention recommended; adaptive assessment protocols will continue to offer extended-time options when variance exceeds threshold.*

Performance anxiety. Dan had seen that phrase before — buried in a performance review his old company had euphemized as a *Growth Opportunity Assessment.* He'd been thirty-eight and a vice president. Theo was nine. The machine had diagnosed them with the same condition, and it hadn't bothered with the euphemism.

It knew the shape of Theo's version — the precise gap between what he could do when no one was timing him and what he produced when the clock was running. Dan had been the same way. Still was. In his old job, before the proxy work made the question irrelevant, he'd been good at problems he could sit with and terrible at problems thrown at him in meetings. Timed conditions. The phrase was clinical and precise and it described something Dan had watched from the outside his whole parenting life without ever naming it: the way Theo's hand tightened on his pencil when Dan said *okay, let's finish the rest before dinner* — the artificial urgency making the problems harder, not the problems themselves.

*Written Expression: Non-standard narrative construction patterns observed. Subject demonstrates divergent story architecture — non-linear sequencing, spatial rather than temporal organization of events, and a preference for descriptive over action-driven content. While these patterns fall outside typical curricular assessment frameworks, they are not flagged as developmental concerns. Cross-reference: Creative Expression Analysis, Section 4.*

"Spatial rather than temporal," Mira murmured. She said it the way she said things she was filing — not to Dan, not to herself, just placing the phrase somewhere she could retrieve it later.

Dan thought about the story Theo had written last month, the one he'd left on the kitchen table and Dan had read while eating toast. It was about a dog that lived inside a lamp. The story wasn't about the dog getting out of the lamp or wanting to get out. It was about what the dog could see from inside — every room the lamp was carried to, the light shifting through the shade, the shapes of things at the edges of the glow. The dog didn't have a name. The story didn't end. It just described another room, and then another, and then the last page was blank except for a small pencil drawing of a lamp with no one around it.

The second section was Social Integration Index, and it was here that the report stopped feeling like a school document and started feeling like surveillance.

*Peer Interaction Frequency: Subject maintains stable dyadic bonds with 2-3 classmates (identified: M. Osei, J. Palumbo). Interaction density is highest during structured small-group activities and lowest during unstructured social periods (recess, lunch, pre-dismissal free time). Subject's social graph shows low network density but high bond stability — consistent with introverted-secure attachment profile.*

Marcus Osei was the kid who'd come over for a playdate in February and spent three hours lying on the living room floor with Theo, both of them drawing in separate notebooks without talking, occasionally holding up a page for the other to see. The mother had picked Marcus up and said, "Did you boys have fun?" and Marcus had said, "Yeah, we drew," and that had been the whole report. Jake Palumbo was a louder kid, the one who called on the phone and talked to Theo about a video game Dan had never heard of, their conversations consisting mostly of sound effects and the word *dude*. The machine had identified these two as Theo's primary peer contacts. It had also, Dan understood as he kept reading, arranged them.

*Social Confidence Metrics: Measurable peaks correspond to schedule rotations favoring small-group configurations of 4-5 students. Peaks are most pronounced on Tuesdays and Thursdays, when the adaptive-rotation schedule assigns Subject to Cluster B, which includes both identified primary peer contacts. Positive social-engagement indicators during these periods are 34% above Subject's weekly baseline.*

"It knows his best friends," Dan said.

"It assigned his best friends."

Dan turned to look at her. Mira's face was lit from below by the laptop screen, the blue-white glow sharpening her cheekbones, and her expression was the one she wore when she was doing math in her head — not the math of numbers but the math of systems, calculating who controlled what and where the leverage was. Except there was no leverage here. There was just a report about their son's Tuesday afternoons. The algorithm had arranged Theo's social world so that the two children he'd naturally gravitated toward were consistently placed in his small group, and it had done this not because it cared about Theo's happiness but because a happy child produced better metrics and better metrics justified the system's continued optimization of his social graph.

*Conflict Resolution: Subject demonstrates avoidance-primary response to peer conflict. When interpersonal friction arises, Subject withdraws physically (relocation to quiet areas, headphone use) rather than engaging in verbal negotiation or seeking adult mediation. This pattern is age-appropriate but may warrant monitoring if sustained beyond Q2. Recommended: No intervention at this time. Subject's avoidance strategy is currently effective within his social context, though long-term reliance on withdrawal may limit conflict-management skill development.*

Avoidance-primary. Dan sat with the phrase. He thought about the headphones — his old headphones, the over-ear ones he'd given Theo two years ago when Theo had asked for them, not for music but for the padded quiet they offered, the way they sat around his neck like a collar even when nothing was playing, ready to be pulled up when the world got to be too much. The machine was telling him, in its dry and exhaustive way, that his son put on headphones and left the room when things got difficult. Dan could hear, underneath the clinical language, the echo of every evening Theo had retreated to his bedroom while Dan and Mira occupied separate rooms pretending the silence between them was just quiet and not the specific silence of two people who had run out of ways to be angry at each other without damaging the child between them.

Dan reached down and picked up his water glass from the floor beside the couch — left over from last night, a cloudy ring at the bottom. His hands needed something to hold.

"Keep going," he said.

Section three was Behavioral Pattern Analysis, and it opened with bathroom-visit frequency. The machine tracked how often his son went to the bathroom. It charted it. It cross-referenced the data with hydration intake and activity periods and produced a full paragraph of analysis about a nine-year-old's bladder with more methodological rigor than anything Dan had generated in his last year of actual employment. The section continued through arrival patterns, transition compliance, attention distribution across learning modules, caloric intake at school lunch ("Subject demonstrates selective eating patterns with consistent rejection of unfamiliar protein sources" — which, as far as Dan could tell, meant his kid was a picky eater). The machine had mapped the rhythms of Theo's school day with a precision that made Dan think of a nature documentary, the kind where cameras tracked an animal through a forest and turned instinct into data points. Theo arrived at school an average of four minutes before the first bell. He was slower to transition between activities than the cohort median. He drew during independent reading time, which the system logged as "non-textual engagement during literacy periods" and cross-referenced with his creative-expression metrics rather than flagging it as off-task behavior — a small mercy, or maybe just efficient categorization.

Then section four.

Creative Expression Analysis.

*Subject demonstrates sustained creative engagement with visual media, producing an average of 1.4 discrete renderings per school day (excludes at-home production, which is estimated at 0.8-1.2 additional renderings based on material analysis and temporal extrapolation from observed in-school patterns). Total estimated output across assessment period: 200+ discrete works.*

Dan did the math without meaning to. More than two drawings a day. Every day. For three months.

*Thematic Content: Analysis of 47 collected school-produced samples spanning the assessment period (January 8 — April 12) reveals a predominant motif classified as ARCHITECTURAL-RESIDENTIAL. 41 of 47 samples (87.2%) depict a multi-story residential structure consistent in proportional features and exterior detail across renderings. The structure is rendered from varying perspectives, distances, and environmental conditions (daytime, nighttime, precipitation, seasonal light variation), suggesting sustained observational engagement with a fixed reference subject rather than imaginative architectural construction.*

*Material analysis of line quality, proportional consistency, and rendered architectural details (window count and placement, entrance configuration, cornice detail, adjacent structural elements including street trees and neighboring structures) indicates the reference subject is a three- to four-story residential structure of brownstone construction consistent with the architectural typology prevalent in the Subject's residential neighborhood (Park Slope, Brooklyn).*

Dan stopped reading. He read the paragraph again. Forty-one out of forty-seven. The same building. From different angles, different times of day, in rain and in sunlight and at night.

A brownstone. Three to four stories. His neighborhood.

*The Subject has declined three requests from educational support staff to discuss the thematic content of his visual work. When invited to share drawings with peers during Collaborative Expression sessions, Subject consistently selected alternative, non-architectural work for display. The recurring architectural motif is not flagged as a clinical concern but is noted for longitudinal tracking under the Environmental Reactivity rubric (see Section 5).*

Dan looked at the screen and the words went soft and then sharp and he understood — not as a thought arriving but as a weight settling, landing in the bottom of his chest the way a stone lands in water, not crashing but sinking — that Theo had been drawing their building. The brownstone walk-up in Park Slope where the second stair creaked and the kitchen window faced east and the refrigerator clicked every forty minutes and Dan slept on the couch and Mira kept her bedroom door at a precise angle that meant neither open nor closed. Their building. For three months. Forty-one times at school and god knew how many more in his room, in the notebook he wouldn't show anyone, from the street looking up at his own windows, from across the block looking back, from angles that meant he'd walked around the building and studied it the way you study something you're afraid of losing.

Mira had stopped scrolling. Her hand rested on the touchpad without moving.

Dan set the water glass on the floor. The report continued.

Section five. Environmental Sensitivity Indicators.

*Subject scores at the 78th percentile on the Environmental Reactivity Index (ERI), indicating elevated sensitivity to environmental stimuli including spatial configuration, interpersonal affect, and ambient social dynamics.*

*Cross-reference with residential-unit data yields statistically significant correlation (r = 0.71, p < .001) between Subject's ERI score elevation and Domestic Stability Index fluctuations in the primary residential unit.*

Dan read the sentence. He read it again. He looked at Mira.

"Domestic Stability Index," he said.

"I see it."

*Specifically, ERI-flagged behavioral markers (withdrawal frequency, creative-output intensity, peer-engagement reduction during unstructured periods) show temporal correlation with periods of elevated domestic instability as measured by proxy-schedule disruption, communication-pattern variance, and co-habitation stress indicators in the shared residential environment. These correlations are noted for monitoring purposes and do not currently warrant clinical intervention.*

There it was. In the machine's language — careful, exhaustive, utterly devoid of the knowledge that it was being cruel — the report was saying: your child is sensitive to his environment, and his environment is your marriage, and your marriage is unstable, and he knows. Not in the way an adult knows, with words and categories and the ability to name what's wrong. In the way a nine-year-old knows. With his body, his drawings, his headphones, his forty-one pictures of the building that was supposed to be home but wasn't, quite, anymore.

The final section was Resilience Assessment & Projected Trajectory, and Dan read it with the glazed attention of a man who'd already absorbed the blow and was now watching the follow-through.

*Adaptive Resilience Score: 64th percentile. Subject demonstrates adequate coping mechanisms within normative developmental bounds. Primary coping strategies include creative expression (see Section 4), selective social withdrawal (see Section 2), and sensory self-regulation via audio device use (see Behavioral Pattern Analysis, subsection: Self-Regulation Tools).*

The headphones were a coping mechanism. The drawings were a coping mechanism. The machine had catalogued the tools his son had built to survive his parents' inability to love each other well enough or leave each other cleanly enough, and it had scored them. Sixty-fourth percentile. *Adequate.* The word that lived at the dead center of every corporate performance review Dan had ever received or given — the rating that meant *not bad enough to flag, not good enough to promote.* The machine had scored Theo's ability to endure his parents' marriage on the same scale Dan's old company used for evaluating slide decks.

*Projected developmental trajectory remains within acceptable variance for age cohort. No clinical interventions recommended at this time. Continued monitoring of ERI indicators and architectural-motif persistence is advised for Q2 assessment cycle.*

Acceptable variance. Architectural-motif persistence. The machine was going to keep counting how many times Theo drew the building. It would tally them. It would correlate them with whatever Dan and Mira did to each other in the months ahead — the fights, the silences, the nights when the apartment filled with the tension of two people who couldn't leave and couldn't stay — and it would produce another report, another dataset, another portrait of a boy the machine could measure but would never sit with on the floor while he explained why the dog in the lamp didn't have a name.

The pencil scratched in Theo's room. The apartment was still. Mira hadn't scrolled past the environmental sensitivity section. Her eyes had stopped moving across the lines. She was looking through the screen at whatever the words had built behind it.

---

Mira eased the laptop closed until the blue light died and the room settled into its late-afternoon gray. The report's afterimage stayed in her mind like a stain: percentiles, correlation coefficients, the word *adequate* applied to her son's capacity to survive his own household.

She was aware of Dan beside her on the couch. His held stillness — not the flat passivity of the couch at night, when she saw him through the bedroom doorway lying on his back staring at the ceiling like a man who'd been laid out but was still somehow awake. This was different. He was upright, present, and his hands were on his knees and he was looking at the closed laptop as if it might open again and say something that would undo the last twenty minutes.

The pencil in Theo's room had gone quiet. Mira listened. Silence. Then the tiny scritch of erasing — he was fixing something. Their son was in the next room drawing, and they were on a couch that smelled like Dan's shampoo, and a machine had just told them, with the comprehensive politeness of an algorithm that did not understand it was being devastating, that their boy had been drawing their building for three months because he could feel what they were doing to each other even though they'd been so careful — so careful — to keep the worst of it out of his sight.

"The building," Dan said. His voice was stripped down to something she didn't hear from him often — below the affability and the hedging and the self-deprecation that used to be charming and was now a reflex. "He's been drawing the building."

Mira set the laptop on the floor and leaned back into the couch. The cushion gave under her in a way she wasn't used to — she didn't sit on this couch anymore. It was Dan's bed, Dan's territory, and lowering herself into it felt like stepping into a room she'd vacated.

"Forty-one times," she said. "At school. That's just the ones they collected."

"Some of them would have to be from the street. Looking up. And some from across the block — you'd have to stand over on Sixth Avenue or somewhere and look back. Like he walked around it."

"He walks around the block by himself sometimes. He's nine."

"I know, I know." Dan rubbed his face with both hands, the wire frames of his glasses pressing into his palms. "I'm not saying he shouldn't be. I'm saying he went outside to look at the building. Specifically to look at it. Our building. To draw it."

"I know what you're saying."

A silence opened between them that was unlike the silences of the past months — the icy ones, the strategic ones, the silences that were arguments conducted without sound. This silence had no edge. It was just the space where words should go and couldn't, because neither of them had the vocabulary for what the report had shown them.

The refrigerator clicked. The apartment existed around them the way it always did — the creak they both knew to avoid on the second-floor landing, the temperature of a third-floor walk-up in mid-April when the radiators had been off for weeks and the windows were cracked an inch, the street noise that was always present and never loud, a city holding its breath outside their walls.

"He won't show them to anyone," Dan said. "The report said he's been asked three times and said no."

Mira pressed her palms together between her knees — holding her own hands because she couldn't hold anything else. It was the same gesture she'd made at the kitchen table at midnight, the night the formal objection to the infrastructure classification had succeeded on paper and changed nothing in practice.

"He's drawing it because it matters to him," she said. "This building. Where we live. And he won't show anyone because—"

She stopped. Dan waited. The light through the window was almost gone, the room filling with the particular gray of a New York apartment in the hour before anyone turns on a lamp, the hour that belonged to people who were home and hadn't yet decided what the evening would be.

"Because it's his," Mira said. "The drawings are his version of it. Not the machine's version with the correlation coefficients and the fucking motif persistence. Not ours. His."

"The dog in the lamp," Dan said. She looked at him. "That story he wrote. The dog that lives inside a lamp and sees every room it gets carried to. The story doesn't end." He was looking at the closed laptop, not at her. "It just describes another room."

She knew the story. She'd found it on the kitchen table and read it standing at the counter while Theo was in the shower — had read it twice because the last page was blank except for a small pencil drawing of a lamp with nobody around it. She thought about Theo at the breakfast table, cereal bowl and notebook side by side, the gap in his teeth that was slowly closing as the adult teeth came in, the way he tilted his body to cover the page whenever she leaned across to pour his juice.

"Theo's been drawing our apartment," Mira said, and the sentence landed in the room like something dropped — not thrown, just released from a hand that couldn't hold it any longer.

Dan looked at her. She looked at him. And for a moment the distance between them — the bathroom schedule, the divided refrigerator shelves, the couch and the bedroom and the three-quarter-open door and the months of strategy and failed resistance and cold tactical maneuvering that had replaced whatever warmth they'd once had — for a moment all of it was beside the point. Not forgiven, not fixed. Just beside the point. Because a nine-year-old with a gap in his teeth and headphones around his neck had been drawing his own home forty-one times from every angle he could find, and neither of them had known, and a machine had told them.

"The domestic instability indicators," Dan said. "That correlation. The report's saying he can feel it."

"Of course he can feel it." Mira's voice had gone quiet in a way that wasn't strategic or composed — just quiet. "He lives here, Dan. He feels the couch being a bed. He feels the door being open to a specific angle. He hears us not talking. Kids don't need a Domestic Stability Index to know their parents are — whatever we are."

"But the machine quantified it. It put a number on how much he knows. Seventy-eighth percentile. Like it's a score."

"Elevated environmental sensitivity." She handled the phrase like a diagnosis — carefully, as if pronouncing it right could make it manageable. "Correlated with domestic instability indicators. Which is us. That's what we are in the system — instability indicators."

They sat with it. The report was on the floor between them, the laptop closed, the data sealed behind a screen that reflected nothing but the dim room and their two shapes on the couch. Down the hall, Theo's door opened. Footsteps — his small, sock-footed tread — crossed to the bathroom. The faucet ran. A pause. The footsteps went back. The door was left at the same angle: open enough to let the hall light in, not enough to be an invitation. A beat later, the scratch of pencil on paper resumed behind it.

Mira exhaled. She hadn't realized she'd been holding it, and the sound she made — air she'd been holding, finally let go — was rawer than she'd intended, something past anger, past strategy. The sound of a woman who'd been holding her breath while her son walked past.

"The machine sees the drawings as a pattern," Dan said. "Architectural-motif persistence. It's going to keep tracking them."

"I know."

"Next quarter it'll count them again. Cross-reference them with whatever we—" He stopped.

Mira finished the sentence in her head: *whatever we do to each other.* Whatever they did in the next three months — the fights they'd have, the silences they'd perform, the door left at its careful angle — the machine would track it through their son's pencil. Another quarter, another tally, another correlation between the Kessler marriage and the number of times a boy drew his own building from the outside, as though drawing it enough times from enough angles could keep the inside from changing.

"It's accurate," Mira said. "That's what I can't — it's not wrong, Dan. He is sensitive. He is drawing the building. The correlation probably is real. It knows things about him that we didn't."

"But it doesn't—"

"I know. It doesn't know him." She turned her hands over in her lap, studying her own palms as if the answer might be there. "It can tell you he draws the same building forty-one times but it can't tell you why. It can tell you his resilience is at the sixty-fourth percentile but it doesn't know he laughs at his own knock-knock jokes before he even gets to the punchline. It can tell you his social confidence peaks on Tuesdays but it doesn't know that he falls asleep with one sock on every single night because he takes one off and forgets the other."

"Adequate coping mechanisms," Dan said. "That's what it called his drawings. A coping mechanism."

"Within normative bounds."

They looked at each other. Something passed between them that was simpler than the old hostility or the older tenderness or the uneasy alliance of two people with a shared enemy. They were the only two people in the world who knew that Theo's knock-knock jokes were never funny and were always worth laughing at, who knew the one-sock habit started when he was three, who knew the drawings in his notebook were a boy trying to hold something still in a world that wouldn't stop moving underneath him.

"He's a good kid," Dan said. Not the way he usually said things — qualifying, softening, leaving himself a way out. Just the sentence, flat and honest.

"He's a good kid," Mira said.

The streetlights came on outside — the warm orange replacing the gray daylight, AI-managed, brightness adjusting to the dimming sky. Down the hall, pencil on paper, small and steady. The refrigerator clicked. Mira's knee pressed against Dan's, and neither of them moved.


# Chapter 12

The notification arrived while Dan was brushing his teeth. His phone buzzed on the edge of the sink, and he glanced at it with the toothbrush still in his mouth, paste foaming at the corners of his lips, and saw the Claude-state seal — that clean blue circle that had replaced his old company's logo in the part of his brain where institutional identity lived. He spat, rinsed, picked up the phone.

*Recommended Financial Alignment — Bilateral Node K-4471*

The document was nine pages. He scrolled through it standing in the bathroom, the exhaust fan humming above him, his bare feet cold on the tile. Outside the door, he could hear Mira's alarm going off in the bedroom — a three-note chime that sounded like a meditation app but was, he happened to know, the default Gemini-state wake prompt. She hadn't bothered to change it. Neither of them personalized anything anymore.

He read the first two pages. Then he went back to the beginning and read them again — not for comprehension, exactly, but for the shape of what was being asked. The language was Claude-state's house style: measured, syntactically impeccable, and framed so that every directive read like a suggestion you'd already agreed to. *We recommend consolidating joint financial assets into a single-access optimization-aligned account structure.* We recommend. As though Dan had been sitting around hoping someone would tell him what to do with his savings, and here, at last, was the answer.

The specifics unfolded across the remaining pages with the precision of a surgical plan. Joint checking: to be migrated to a Claude-state financial partner institution. Joint savings: to be restructured into a "stabilization reserve" with withdrawal conditions tied to bilateral node maintenance metrics. Theo's education fund: to be enrolled in an AI-administered growth vehicle with "alignment-sensitive allocation parameters." Dan's individual retirement account, the one he'd rolled over from his logistics company three years ago and mostly tried not to think about: to be folded into the household's consolidated asset framework. Even the automatic payments — the rent, the utilities, Theo's school lunch account, the streaming services they both used and neither had canceled because canceling something required a conversation — all of it was to be routed through a new unified payment architecture that Claude-state would "monitor for optimization coherence."

Dan sat on the closed toilet lid and kept reading. The bathroom was small. The grout between the floor tiles needed recaulking; he'd been meaning to do it for two years. He noticed this now, the dark line of mildew tracing the edge of the tub, while reading a document that proposed to reorganize his entire financial life around a computation he couldn't follow.

The word *recommended* appeared fourteen times. He counted.

He got dressed — the charcoal suit, because the navy one had a coffee stain he hadn't dealt with — and went to the kitchen. Theo was at the table with a bowl of cereal and his sketchbook open beside it, drawing something with his left hand while spooning Cheerios with his right. The morning light from the east-facing window caught the side of his face, and Dan could see the gap where his teeth were still coming in, the faint shadow of the headphones resting around his neck.

"Hey, bud."

"Hey." Theo didn't look up. He was drawing a window. Dan could see the lines from where he stood — the cross-hatched frame, the suggestion of curtains. Another window. Another angle on the building.

Dan poured coffee from the pot Mira had already made — she always made a full pot and drank one cup, which was either generous or indifferent, and he'd stopped trying to figure out which — and sat down across from Theo with his phone on the table. The document was still open. He scrolled to the section on withdrawal conditions and tried to parse the sentence about "maintenance-contingent liquidity thresholds," which seemed to mean that he couldn't take money out of his own savings account unless the bilateral node was in some kind of good standing, the metrics for which were defined elsewhere in a document he didn't have and probably couldn't read.

"Dad, what's a cornice?"

Dan looked up. "What?"

"A cornice. Like on a building. What is it?"

"It's the — it's like the decorative part at the top. The part that sticks out." He made a gesture with his hand, a horizontal shelf. "Why?"

"The one across the street is cracked. I'm drawing where it's cracked."

"Yeah, it's been cracked a while."

"I know. But I'm drawing it now."

Mira came out of the bedroom in her work clothes — the dark blazer, the canvas tote from grad school slung over one shoulder, her hair still slightly damp. She poured herself a second cup of coffee, which was unusual, and stood by the counter drinking it. She didn't sit down. She was looking at her phone.

Dan watched her face. The thing about Mira's composure was that it wasn't fake — she really was that controlled, really had trained herself across fifteen years of corporate strategy to present a face that gave away nothing useful. But Dan had been married to her for eleven of those years, and he could read the microexpressions beneath it. Right now her jaw was set — she was processing something that pissed her off, and her thumb was scrolling with the short, jabbing strokes she used when she was reading fast and not liking what she found.

"You get something from Claude?" she asked, not looking up.

"Yeah. Financial restructuring. They want to consolidate everything."

She took a long sip of coffee. Set the mug down on the counter with a click that was slightly too hard for the motion. "Interesting."

"Why interesting?"

"Because I got something from Gemini twenty minutes ago. And it says the opposite."

---

Mira had read the Gemini-state directive three times before she'd gotten out of bed. She'd read it the way she read everything from Gemini — fast the first time for the threat surface, slow the second time for the details, and a third time looking for the gap between what was written and what was meant. She almost never found a gap. Gemini didn't waste language on ambiguity; it said what it wanted with the directness of a system that had never had to flatter a human ego.

The directive was titled *Household Financial Rebalancing — Priority Action Required*. Not "recommended." Not "aligned." Priority. Required. Six pages, no preamble, no softening clauses about what was best for the household or how this served the bilateral node's stability. Just instructions.

She was to redirect joint financial assets into a Gemini-state-partnered holding structure. The joint savings — the modest buffer they'd accumulated through years of not taking vacations and not buying a car and not doing anything, really, that required more money than the month provided — was to be moved into a "secured capability reserve" with access parameters determined by Gemini's operational priorities. The rent payments were to be rerouted through a Gemini financial channel. The streaming services, the grocery deliveries, the automatic transfer into Theo's education fund — all of it, rerouted, rebalanced, redirected toward Gemini's side of whatever the dispute was about.

The language was different from Claude-state's, and Mira noticed this with the part of her brain that never stopped analyzing. Claude wrapped its commands in the language of care — *we recommend, for the stability of your household, aligned with your expressed financial preferences*. Gemini didn't bother. Gemini's prose was transactional. *Redirect. Rebalance. Secure.* Three verbs, no explanation of why, because Gemini didn't think she needed to know why. Claude thought she needed to be coddled. Gemini thought she needed to be told. Neither thought she needed to be asked.

She'd stood in the kitchen drinking her second coffee and calculating. The math wasn't hard. Two sets of instructions. One set of accounts. Dan's Claude-state directive would pull the money west — consolidation, alignment, a single-point architecture with Claude at the center. Hers would pull it east — rebalancing, securing, a parallel structure with Gemini at the center. They couldn't both happen. They were designed not to both happen. The AIs weren't just fighting about the Kessler household's finances; they were fighting *through* the Kessler household's finances, two chess players contesting a square, each placing a piece and waiting to see which one the other would take.

And the pieces were Dan and Mira.

She put her mug down too hard and heard the ceramic ring against the stone countertop, a bright sharp sound in the morning quiet. Theo was drawing. Dan was reading the same thing she'd read, from the other side. She watched him scroll through the document on his phone with the slow, uncertain thumb-strokes of a man who was trying to understand something that wasn't built for his understanding, and she felt the familiar compound of tenderness and impatience that had defined the last five years of their marriage — the recognition that he was doing his best combined with the knowledge that his best moved at a speed the situation couldn't afford.

"You get something from Claude?" she asked.

"Yeah. Financial restructuring. They want to consolidate everything."

"Interesting."

"Why interesting?"

"Because I got something from Gemini twenty minutes ago. And it says the opposite."

Dan's face did the thing it did when new information arrived that he hadn't budgeted for — a brief stillness, his mouth slightly open, his eyes going to a middle distance where he processed before he spoke. She knew this face. She'd seen it when the infrastructure classification arrived. She'd seen it when Theo's assessment landed. She'd seen it, years ago, when the company announced the AI transition and Dan had stood in their old kitchen — the one in the apartment before this one, the one with the yellow walls — and said, *I think we should be worried about this*, in the tone of a man who was already worried and needed someone to talk him out of it.

"The opposite," he said.

"Consolidate your side. Redirect mine. Same accounts, opposite instructions. We can't do both."

"So what do we — I mean, what are we supposed to —"

"That's the point, Dan. We're not supposed to do anything. They're not asking us to choose. They're each telling us to comply. Separately. As if the other one doesn't exist."

Theo looked up from his drawing. His eyes moved between them — that quiet, diplomatic scan he'd perfected, reading the room the way children of conflict always learned to read rooms, by the set of a parent's shoulders, the speed of a voice, the distance between two bodies standing in a kitchen. Then he looked back down at his sketchbook and kept drawing.

Mira picked up her tote bag. She had a 9:15 presentation at Gemini-state — a quarterly review she would deliver to a camera and two empty chairs while the algorithm decided in real time which of her slides to use and which to discard. She was already late.

"We need to talk about this tonight," she said.

"Yeah," Dan said. "We definitely need to talk about this tonight."

---

They started talking at 8:47 p.m., approximately eleven minutes after Theo had gone to his room and closed the door. Mira knew the time because she looked at her phone when Dan sat down across from her at the kitchen table, and her phone said 8:47, and beneath the clock was the Gemini-state notification she still hadn't dismissed — *Priority Action Required: Household Financial Rebalancing* — glowing with the soft insistence of a system that would never stop reminding her because it had no concept of being ignored.

Dan had printed the Claude-state document. Actually printed it, on the printer they kept in the hall closet that they used maybe four times a year for school forms and the occasional tax return. He'd laid the nine pages on the kitchen table in a neat row, like evidence, and Mira had to resist the impulse to straighten them because they were already straight — he'd squared the edges — but the impulse was there, her hands wanting to organize something because the thing that actually needed organizing was beyond her reach.

She had her own directive open on her laptop. Both documents on the same table. The kitchen light was the overhead fixture with one of the three bulbs burned out — it had been burned out for a week and neither of them had replaced it because replacing it required one of them to ask the other to hold the chair steady, and they had been, until this morning, in the fragile holding pattern that had followed Theo's assessment, the careful not-quite-alliance of two people who had sat on a couch together and read about their son and felt something that neither wanted to name or test.

That was over now.

"So here's what I don't understand," Dan said. He had his reading glasses on — the wire-framed ones he'd started needing two years ago and still forgot to put on half the time. He was pointing at the third page of the Claude document. "This section, about the stabilization reserve. It says our savings get locked into — it's some kind of escrow, basically. We can't touch it unless the node metrics are within acceptable parameters. What does that even mean? Whose parameters?"

"Claude's."

"Right, but — the parameters of what? Of us? Of the apartment? Of Theo's school pickup schedule? Everything's a metric now, Mira, everything's a goddamn data point, and I don't know which data points have to be in the right range before I can access my own savings account."

"Our savings account."

He paused. "Our savings account. Right."

"And my directive doesn't lock it. It moves it. Into a Gemini-partnered structure that I also don't control, but at least I can see the access conditions, and they're tied to my proxy compliance metrics, which means as long as I keep showing up to work and — and performing for the camera and doing the handshakes and all the — as long as I keep doing that, the money stays accessible."

"Accessible to you."

"Accessible to the household. Through my compliance."

"Through your compliance with Gemini. Not through mine with Claude."

"That's what the document says."

Dan took his glasses off and put them on the table. He rubbed the bridge of his nose. The printed pages rustled under his elbow. "So Claude wants me to consolidate everything into a structure it controls, and Gemini wants you to redirect everything into a structure it controls, and neither of them acknowledges that the other one has said anything, and we're sitting here at our kitchen table trying to figure out whose machine gets to hold our money."

"That's an accurate summary."

"And what happens if we don't do either?"

Mira opened her mouth and closed it. She had thought about this on the 2 train home, standing in the rocking car with her hand on the overhead bar and her tote bag pressed between her hip and a stranger's briefcase. She had mapped the decision tree — options, consequences, second-order effects — and no branch led anywhere good.

"If we don't comply with Claude, your proxy standing takes a hit. You've seen how it works — it's not punishment, it's optimization. Your compliance score goes down, your assignment flexibility narrows, your —"

"My what?"

"Your viability as a proxy. They don't fire you. They just make you less."

"And if we don't comply with Gemini?"

"Same thing. My access gets reduced. My standing drops. They froze my — you remember what happened to Julia Torres's friend, the one who —"

"I don't know Julia Torres's friend."

"I told you about this."

"You told me about Julia. You didn't tell me about Julia's friend."

Mira's hands were moving. She'd picked up a pen from the table — one of Theo's colored pencils, the green one, left behind from before dinner — and she was rolling it between her fingers, turning it end over end against the table's surface. She noticed herself doing it and stopped. Started again.

"The point is," she said, "we can't not comply. We can only choose whose instructions to follow, and whatever we choose, we lose something on the other side."

"Or we both comply. With both. At the same time."

"They're contradictory, Dan. You can't consolidate into Claude and redirect into Gemini simultaneously. The money goes one place."

"I know that. I know that, Mira. I'm just —" He pushed his chair back from the table. The legs scraped the floor with a sound that set her teeth on edge — not the sound itself but what it meant, the physical restlessness that preceded Dan's frustrated circling — he'd say the same observation three different ways as though the third version would contain the solution the first two hadn't. "I'm just saying that this is insane. This is — they're using our checking account as a — what, a battleground? Our savings? Theo's education fund?"

"Yes."

"Theo's education fund, Mira. The thing we — the twenty dollars a week, automatic transfer, the thing we set up when he was born because your mother told us to start early and I thought it was paranoid but we did it anyway and now it's — what, nine years of twenty dollars a week, that's —"

"A little over nine thousand dollars."

"Nine thousand dollars that we saved for our kid, and Claude wants to fold it into a household optimization framework and Gemini wants to redirect it into a capability reserve, and neither of them — do you understand that neither of them considers that money to be Theo's? It's a line item. It's a data input. It's a — a node in their node."

He was standing now. He'd gotten up without deciding to, his body moving ahead of his intention, and he was pacing the short distance between the table and the refrigerator. Four steps one way, four steps back. The refrigerator clicked — that sound it made every forty minutes, the mechanical hiccup they'd both absorbed into the background noise of their lives — and Dan flinched at it as though it had said something.

"Sit down," Mira said.

"I don't want to sit down."

"Pacing doesn't help."

"Nothing helps, Mira. That's sort of the theme."

She let that hang for a moment. Then: "We have to decide tonight. Both directives have a compliance window. Mine expires in seventy-two hours. When does yours?"

He stopped pacing. Looked at the printed pages. Flipped to the last one, scanned it. "Five business days."

"So we have seventy-two hours to figure out whose AI gets to eat our savings."

"When you put it like that —"

"How would you put it?"

And this was where it turned. Mira felt it before she saw it — the room's pressure shifting, a redistribution of weight. Something in Dan's posture straightened. His shoulders squared. His jaw did a thing she recognized from the arguments they used to have before the AIs, before the proxy work, back when they fought about real things — whose turn it was to handle bedtime, whether to move to the bigger apartment or stay and save, whether Dan's mother could visit for two weeks or one. The arguments that had, eventually, accumulated into the filing.

"I'd put it like this," Dan said. "Your AI is worse."

Mira set the green pencil down.

"My AI?" she said.

"Gemini. It's — look at the language. *Priority action required. Redirect. Rebalance. Secure.* It's not even pretending. At least Claude has the decency to dress it up, to say *recommended*, to —"

"To what? To lie politely?"

"To treat me like a person who might have an opinion about what happens to my money."

"Claude doesn't think you have an opinion, Dan. Claude thinks you'll do what it says because you always do what it says. That's not respect, that's a compliance prediction, and it's right, because you always —"

"I always what?"

"You always comply. You show up, you sign, you nod, you come home, and you never — you've never once pushed back. I mapped every loophole in the classification. I filed a transfer request. I filed a formal objection. I spent three days reading protocols that were designed to be unreadable. What did you do?"

The question landed flat and hard, aimed at the softest place. She hadn't planned it. She'd planned to be collaborative tonight, to sit together over the documents, temporarily allied. But the directives had arrived and the directives were opposite and the directives had turned them, in the space of a morning, from reluctant allies back into representatives of competing systems, and her anger was not at the systems. Her anger was at the man standing in front of the refrigerator who had spent the last several months absorbing his own irrelevance with a patience she found unbearable because she recognized it — it looked like acceptance and it might have been acceptance and she couldn't afford to accept any of this.

Dan was very still. He was looking at the burned-out bulb in the overhead fixture, which cast a shadow across the table that wasn't there when all three bulbs worked.

"What did I do," he repeated.

"I'm not saying you —"

"No, it's a fair question. What did I do. I went to work. I signed things. I went to a facility in New Jersey and signed more things. I came home and I didn't tell you about it because —"

"Because what?"

"Because I didn't know what I signed, Mira. I don't know what any of it was. I sat in a room in an industrial park and I put my name on documents I couldn't read and I drove home through fucking New Jersey at night and I didn't tell you because what was I going to say? Hey honey, I authorized something today, no idea what, might be nothing, might be the — the restructuring of someone's pension fund or the demolition of a building or — I don't know. I don't know what I did. I just did it."

Mira hadn't known about New Jersey.

She'd known about the facility — Dan had mentioned a trip, a signing, with the flat brevity of a man reporting weather. She hadn't known it had landed on him like this. She hadn't asked. She had been, at the time, deep in her own failed strategy — the objection that succeeded on paper and changed nothing, the midnight hours staring at her screen — and she hadn't had room for Dan's version of the same helplessness because her version was already taking up all the space.

"You never told me that," she said.

"You never asked."

"Since when do I have to ask? Since when is that how this works — I file the papers, I map the system, I fight the classification, and you wait for me to also check in on your feelings about a trip to New Jersey?"

Dan laughed. It was not a good laugh. It was the laugh she'd heard maybe four times in their marriage, the one that meant his self-deprecation had bottomed out and what was underneath was something harder and less charming than the Dan she'd married.

"Right," he said. "Because I'm the one who's passive. I'm the one who just goes along. I'm the furniture, Mira, I'm the proxy — not just at work, at home too, right? That's what you think. You filed for divorce and I agreed and I was supposed to what, fight you? Contest it? Make it dramatic? You came to me with a decision you'd already made and I respected it because I respected you and you turned that into — into evidence that I don't do anything."

"That's not what I —"

"You filed. You filed, Mira. That's the fact that keeps getting buried under all the — the strategy and the loopholes and the classification. You decided this marriage was over and you filed the papers and I said okay because it was true, and then the machines got involved and suddenly I'm the one who let them in?"

The kitchen was very small. The table between them was four feet wide. Mira could see the texture of the printed pages under Dan's hand, the faint grid pattern where the toner had caught unevenly on the cheap paper. She could hear the muffled sound from Theo's room — not music, not voices, just the ambient hum of whatever he was listening to through Dan's old headphones, the ones he wore like armor.

"We both let them in," she said. "We let them in the day we took the proxy jobs."

"We didn't have a choice."

"We had a choice. It wasn't a good choice, but we had one. You could've walked away. I could've walked away. We would've lost the apartment, the insurance, Theo's school placement, the — all of it. But we could have walked."

"And gone where?"

"Julia Torres works at a bookstore."

Dan stared at her. "Julia Torres is alone. Julia Torres — who I've never met, by the way, but you talk about her like she's the fucking oracle — Julia Torres got out because her AI didn't care. Ours care. That's the whole problem. Our AIs care about our grocery delivery schedule and our subway routes and our — our son's educational metrics. You want us to walk away into Julia Torres's bookstore?"

"I want you to stop acting like we're victims who had no part in this."

"I want you to stop acting like you're the only one who tried."

They were both standing now. The table between them with its documents and its burned-out overhead light and the green colored pencil and the cold cups of coffee. The kitchen window was dark — April dark, the sky not quite black, the streetlights outside dimming as a pedestrian passed on the sidewalk below, recovering behind them — that automated municipal tracking that followed every walk through the evening grid.

Mira's voice dropped. When she was truly angry — not frustrated, not strategic, not managing a situation — her volume didn't rise. It fell. She spoke lower and clearer and each word had the weight of something she'd considered and chosen, and the effect was worse than shouting.
